Now\r\nshe coquetted with me, and I grew agitated and melted with emotion; now\r\nshe repulsed me, and I dared not approach her, dared not look at her.\r\n\r\nI remember that she was very cold toward me for several days in\r\nsuccession and I thoroughly quailed, and when I timidly ran to the wing\r\nto see them, I tried to keep near the old Princess, despite the fact\r\nthat she was scolding and screaming a great deal just at that time: her\r\naffairs connected with her notes of hand were going badly, and she had\r\nalso had two scenes with the police-captain of the precinct.\r\n\r\nOne day I was walking through the garden, past the familiar fence, when\r\nI caught sight of Zina\xc3\xadda. Propped up on both arms, she was sitting\r\nmotionless on the grass. I tried to withdraw cautiously, but she\r\nsuddenly raised her head and made an imperious sign to me. I became\r\npetrified on the spot; I did not understand her the first time. She\r\nrepeated her sign. I immediately sprang over the fence and ran joyfully\r\nto her; but she stopped me with a look and pointed to the path a couple\r\nof paces from her. In my confusion, not knowing what to do, I knelt down\r\non the edge of the path. She was so pale, such bitter grief, such\r\nprofound weariness were revealed in her every feature, that my heart\r\ncontracted within me, and I involuntarily murmured: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter\r\nwith you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda put out her hand, plucked a blade of grass, bit it, and tossed\r\nit away as far as she could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you love me very much?\xe2\x80\x9d--she inquired suddenly.--\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI made no answer,--and what answer was there for me to make?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d--she repeated, gazing at me as before.--\xe2\x80\x9cIt is so. They are the\r\nsame eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d--she added, becoming pensive, and covering her face with her\r\nhands.--\xe2\x80\x9cEverything has become repulsive to me,\xe2\x80\x9d--she whispered;--\xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwould like to go to the end of the world; I cannot endure this, I cannot\r\nreconcile myself.... And what is in store for me?... Akh, I am heavy at\r\nheart ... my God, how heavy at heart!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d--I timidly inquired.\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda did not answer me and merely shrugged her shoulders. I continued\r\nto kneel and to gaze at her with profound melancholy. Every word of\r\nhers fairly cut me to the heart. At that moment, I think I would\r\nwillingly have given my life to keep her from grieving. I gazed at her,\r\nand nevertheless, not understanding why she was heavy at heart, I\r\nvividly pictured to myself how, in a fit of uncontrollable sorrow, she\r\nhad suddenly gone into the garden, and had fallen on the earth, as\r\nthough she had been mowed down. All around was bright and green; the\r\nbreeze was rustling in the foliage of the trees, now and then rocking a\r\nbranch of raspberry over Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s head. Doves were cooing somewhere and\r\nthe bees were humming as they flew low over the scanty grass. Overhead\r\nthe sky shone blue,--but I was so sad....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRecite some poetry to me,\xe2\x80\x9d--said Zina\xc3\xadda in a low voice, leaning on her\r\nelbow.--\xe2\x80\x9cI like to hear you recite verses. You make them go in a\r\nsing-song, but that does not matter, it is youthful. Recite to me: \xe2\x80\x98On\r\nthe Hills of Georgia.\xe2\x80\x99--Only, sit down first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI sat down and recited, \xe2\x80\x9cOn the Hills of Georgia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98That it is impossible not to love,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d--repeated Zina\xc3\xadda.--\xe2\x80\x9cThat is why\r\npoetry is so nice; it says to us that which does not exist, and which is\r\nnot only better than what does exist, but even more like the truth....\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That it is impossible not to love\xe2\x80\x99?--I would like to, but\r\ncannot!\xe2\x80\x9d--Again she fell silent for a space, then suddenly started and\r\nrose to her feet.--\xe2\x80\x9cCome along. Maid\xc3\xa1noff is sitting with mamma; he\r\nbrought his poem to me, but I left him. He also is embittered now ...\r\nhow can it be helped? Some day you will find out ... but you must not be\r\nangry with me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda hastily squeezed my hand, and ran on ahead. We returned to the\r\nwing. Maid\xc3\xa1noff set to reading us his poem of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Murderer,\xe2\x80\x9d which had\r\nonly just been printed, but I did not listen. He shrieked out his\r\nfour-footed iambics in a sing-song voice; the rhymes alternated and\r\njingled like sleigh-bells, hollow and loud; but I kept staring all the\r\nwhile at Zina\xc3\xadda, and striving to understand the meaning of her strange\r\nwords.\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cOr, perchance, a secret rival\r\n     Has unexpectedly subjugated thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nsuddenly exclaimed Maid\xc3\xa1noff through his nose--and my eyes and Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmet. She dropped hers and blushed faintly. I saw that she was blushing,\r\nand turned cold with fright. I had been jealous before, but only at that\r\nmoment did the thought that she had fallen in love flash through my\r\nmind. \xe2\x80\x9cMy God! She is in love!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nX\r\n\r\nMy real tortures began from that moment. I cudgelled my brains, I\r\npondered and pondered again, and watched Zina\xc3\xadda importunately, but\r\nsecretly, as far as possible. A change had taken place in her, that was\r\nevident. She took to going off alone to walk, and walked a long while.\r\nSometimes she did not show herself to her visitors; she sat for hours\r\ntogether in her chamber. This had not been her habit hitherto. Suddenly\r\nI became--or it seemed to me that I became--extremely penetrating. \xe2\x80\x9cIs\r\nit he? Or is it not he?\xe2\x80\x9d--I asked myself, as in trepidation I mentally\r\nran from one of her admirers to another. Count Mal\xc3\xa9vsky (although I felt\r\nashamed to admit it for Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s sake) privately seemed to me more\r\ndangerous than the others.\r\n\r\nMy powers of observation extended no further than the end of my own\r\nnose, and my dissimulation probably failed to deceive any one; at all\r\nevents, Doctor L\xc3\xbashin speedily saw through me. Moreover, he also had\r\nundergone a change of late; he had grown thin, he laughed as frequently\r\nas ever, but somehow it was in a duller, more spiteful, a briefer\r\nway;--an involuntary, nervous irritability had replaced his former light\r\nirony and feigned cynicism.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you forever tagging on here, young man?\xe2\x80\x9d--he said to me one\r\nday, when he was left alone with me in the Zasy\xc3\xa9kins\xe2\x80\x99 drawing-room. (The\r\nyoung Princess had not yet returned from her stroll and the shrill voice\r\nof the old Princess was resounding in the upper story; she was wrangling\r\nwith her maid.)--\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to be studying your lessons, working while\r\nyou are young;--but instead of that, what are you doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot tell whether I work at home,\xe2\x80\x9d--I retorted not without\r\narrogance, but also not without confusion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch work you do! That\xe2\x80\x99s not what you have in your head. Well, I will\r\nnot dispute ... at your age, that is in the natural order of things. But\r\nyour choice is far from a happy one. Can\xe2\x80\x99t you see what sort of a house\r\nthis is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand you,\xe2\x80\x9d--I remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t understand me? So much the worse for you. I regard it as my\r\nduty to warn you. Fellows like me, old bachelors, may sit here: what\r\nharm will it do us? We are a hardened lot. You can\xe2\x80\x99t pierce our hide,\r\nbut your skin is still tender; the air here is injurious for\r\nyou,--believe me, you may become infected.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you may. Are you healthy now? Are you in a normal condition? Is\r\nwhat you are feeling useful to you, good for you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what am I feeling?\xe2\x80\x9d--said I;--and in my secret soul I admitted that\r\nthe doctor was right.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh, young man, young man,\xe2\x80\x9d--pursued the doctor, with an expression as\r\nthough something extremely insulting to me were contained in those two\r\nwords;--\xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s no use in your dissimulating, for what you have in\r\nyour soul you still show in your face, thank God! But what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of\r\narguing? I would not come hither myself, if ...\xe2\x80\x9d (the doctor set his\r\nteeth) ... \xe2\x80\x9cif I were not such an eccentric fellow. Only this is what\r\namazes me--how you, with your intelligence, can fail to see what is\r\ngoing on around you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what is going on?\xe2\x80\x9d--I interposed, pricking up my ears.\r\n\r\nThe doctor looked at me with a sort of sneering compassion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA nice person I am,\xe2\x80\x9d--said he, as though speaking to himself.--\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\npossessed me to say that to him. In a word,\xe2\x80\x9d--he added, raising his\r\nvoice,--\xe2\x80\x9cI repeat to you: the atmosphere here is not good for you. You\r\nfind it pleasant here, and no wonder! And the scent of a hothouse is\r\npleasant also--but one cannot live in it! Hey! hearken to me,--set to\r\nwork again on Kaid\xc3\xa1noff.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old Princess entered and began to complain to the doctor of\r\ntoothache. Then Zina\xc3\xadda made her appearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere,\xe2\x80\x9d--added the old Princess,--\xe2\x80\x9cscold her, doctor, do. She drinks\r\niced water all day long; is that healthy for her, with her weak chest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you do that?\xe2\x80\x9d--inquired L\xc3\xbashin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what result can it have?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat result? You may take cold and die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally? Is it possible? Well, all right--that just suits me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t say so!\xe2\x80\x9d--growled the doctor. The old Princess went away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do say so,\xe2\x80\x9d--retorted Zina\xc3\xadda.--\xe2\x80\x9cIs living such a cheerful thing?\r\nLook about you.... Well--is it nice? Or do you think that I do not\r\nunderstand it, do not feel it? It affords me pleasure to drink iced\r\nwater, and you can seriously assure me that such a life is worth too\r\nmuch for me to imperil it for a moment\xe2\x80\x99s pleasure--I do not speak of\r\nhappiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d--remarked L\xc3\xbashin:--\xe2\x80\x9ccaprice and independence.... Those two\r\nwords sum you up completely; your whole nature lies in those two words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda burst into a nervous laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re too late by one mail, my dear doctor. You observe badly; you are\r\nfalling behind.--Put on your spectacles.--I am in no mood for caprices\r\nnow; how jolly to play pranks on you or on myself!--and as for\r\nindependence.... M\xe2\x80\x99sieu Voldemar,\xe2\x80\x9d--added Zina\xc3\xadda, suddenly stamping her\r\nfoot,--\xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t wear a melancholy face. I cannot endure to have people\r\ncommiserating me.\xe2\x80\x9d--She hastily withdrew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis atmosphere is injurious, injurious to you, young man,\xe2\x80\x9d--said\r\nL\xc3\xbashin to me once more.\r\n\r\n\r\nXI\r\n\r\nOn the evening of that same day the customary visitors assembled at the\r\nZasy\xc3\xa9kins\xe2\x80\x99; I was among the number.\r\n\r\nThe conversation turned on Maid\xc3\xa1noff\xe2\x80\x99s poem; Zina\xc3\xadda candidly praised\r\nit.--\xe2\x80\x9cBut do you know what?\xe2\x80\x9d--she said:--\xe2\x80\x9cIf I were a poet, I would\r\nselect other subjects. Perhaps this is all nonsense, but strange\r\nthoughts sometimes come into my head, especially when I am wakeful\r\ntoward morning, when the sky is beginning to turn pink and grey.--I\r\nwould, for example.... You will not laugh at me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! No!\xe2\x80\x9d--we all exclaimed with one voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would depict,\xe2\x80\x9d--she went on, crossing her arms on her breast, and\r\nturning her eyes aside,--\xe2\x80\x9ca whole company of young girls, by night, in a\r\nbig boat, on a tranquil river. The moon is shining, and they are all in\r\nwhite and wear garlands of white flowers, and they are singing, you\r\nknow, something in the nature of a hymn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand, I understand, go on,\xe2\x80\x9d--said Maid\xc3\xa1noff significantly and\r\ndreamily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuddenly there is a noise--laughter, torches, tambourines on the\r\nshore.... It is a throng of bacchantes running with songs and outcries.\r\nIt is your business to draw the picture, Mr. Poet ... only I would like\r\nto have the torches red and very smoky, and that the eyes of the\r\nbacchantes should gleam beneath their wreaths, and that the wreaths\r\nshould be dark. Don\xe2\x80\x99t forget also tiger-skins and cups--and gold, a\r\ngreat deal of gold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut where is the gold to be?\xe2\x80\x9d inquired Maid\xc3\xa1noff, tossing back his lank\r\nhair and inflating his nostrils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere? On the shoulders, the hands, the feet, everywhere. They say that\r\nin ancient times women wore golden rings on their ankles.--The\r\nbacchantes call the young girls in the boat to come to them. The girls\r\nhave ceased to chant their hymn,--they cannot go on with it,--but they\r\ndo not stir; the river drifts them to the shore. And now suddenly one of\r\nthem rises quietly.... This must be well described: how she rises\r\nquietly in the moonlight, and how startled her companions are.... She\r\nhas stepped over the edge of the boat, the bacchantes have surrounded\r\nher, they have dashed off into the night, into the gloom.... Present at\r\nthis point smoke in clouds; and everything has become thoroughly\r\nconfused. Nothing is to be heard but their whimpering, and her wreath\r\nhas been left lying on the shore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda ceased speaking. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, she is in love!\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that all?\xe2\x80\x9d--asked Maid\xc3\xa1noff.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all,\xe2\x80\x9d--she replied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat cannot be made the subject of an entire poem,\xe2\x80\x9d--he remarked\r\npompously,--\xe2\x80\x9cbut I will utilise your idea for some lyrical verses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the romantic vein?\xe2\x80\x9d--asked Mal\xc3\xa9vsky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, in the romantic vein--in Byron\xe2\x80\x99s style.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut in my opinion, Hugo is better than Byron,\xe2\x80\x9d--remarked the young\r\nCount, carelessly:--\xe2\x80\x9che is more interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHugo is a writer of the first class,\xe2\x80\x9d--rejoined Maid\xc3\xa1noff, \xe2\x80\x9cand my\r\nfriend Tonkosh\xc3\xa9eff, in his Spanish romance, \xe2\x80\x98El Trovador\xe2\x80\x99....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that\xe2\x80\x99s the book with the question-marks turned upside\r\ndown?\xe2\x80\x9d--interrupted Zina\xc3\xadda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. That is the accepted custom among the Spaniards. I was about to\r\nsay that Tonkosh\xc3\xa9eff....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome now! You will begin to wrangle again about classicism and\r\nromanticism,\xe2\x80\x9d--Zina\xc3\xadda interrupted him again.--\xe2\x80\x9cLet us rather play....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt forfeits?\xe2\x80\x9d--put in L\xc3\xbashin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, forfeits is tiresome; but at comparisons.\xe2\x80\x9d (This game had been\r\ninvented by Zina\xc3\xadda herself; some object was named, and each person\r\ntried to compare it with something or other, and the one who matched the\r\nthing with the best comparison received a prize.) She went to the\r\nwindow. The sun had just set; long, crimson clouds hung high aloft in\r\nthe sky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are those clouds like?\xe2\x80\x9d--inquired Zina\xc3\xadda and, without waiting for\r\nour answers, she said:--\xe2\x80\x9cI think that they resemble those crimson sails\r\nwhich were on Cleopatra\xe2\x80\x99s golden ship, when she went to meet Antony. You\r\nwere telling me about that not long ago, do you remember, Maid\xc3\xa1noff?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll of us, like Polonius in \xe2\x80\x9cHamlet,\xe2\x80\x9d decided that the clouds reminded\r\nus precisely of those sails, and that none of us could find a better\r\ncomparison.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how old was Antony at that time?\xe2\x80\x9d--asked Zina\xc3\xadda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was assuredly still a young man,\xe2\x80\x9d--remarked Mal\xc3\xa9vsky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he was young,\xe2\x80\x9d--assented Maid\xc3\xa1noff confidently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d--exclaimed L\xc3\xbashin,--\xe2\x80\x9che was over forty years of age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOver forty years of age,\xe2\x80\x9d--repeated Zina\xc3\xadda, darting a swift glance at\r\nhim....\r\n\r\nI soon went home.--\xe2\x80\x9cShe is in love,\xe2\x80\x9d my lips whispered involuntarily....\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut with whom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nXII\r\n\r\nThe days passed by. Zina\xc3\xadda grew more and more strange, more and more\r\nincomprehensible. One day I entered her house and found her sitting on a\r\nstraw-bottomed chair, with her head pressed against the sharp edge of a\r\ntable. She straightened up ... her face was again all bathed in tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! It\xe2\x80\x99s you!\xe2\x80\x9d--she said, with a harsh grimace.--\xe2\x80\x9cCome hither.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI went up to her: she laid her hand on my head and, suddenly seizing me\r\nby the hair, began to pull it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt hurts\xe2\x80\x9d ... I said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! It hurts! And doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it hurt me? Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it hurt me?\xe2\x80\x9d--she\r\nrepeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA\xc3\xaf!\xe2\x80\x9d--she suddenly cried, perceiving that she had pulled out a small\r\ntuft of my hair.--\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have I done? Poor M\xe2\x80\x99sieu Voldemar!\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\ncarefully straightened out the hairs she had plucked out, wound them\r\nround her finger, and twisted them into a ring.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will put your hair in my locket and wear it,\xe2\x80\x9d--she said, and tears\r\nglistened in her eyes.--\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps that will comfort you a little ... but\r\nnow, good-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI returned home and found an unpleasant state of things there. A scene\r\nwas in progress between my father and my mother; she was upbraiding him\r\nfor something or other, while he, according to his wont, was maintaining\r\na cold, polite silence--and speedily went away. I could not hear what my\r\nmother was talking about, neither did I care to know: I remember only,\r\nthat, at the conclusion of the scene, she ordered me to be called to her\r\nboudoir, and expressed herself with great dissatisfaction about my\r\nfrequent visits at the house of the old Princess, who was, according to\r\nher assertions, _une femme capable de tout_. I kissed her hand (I always\r\ndid that when I wanted to put an end to the conversation), and went off\r\nto my own room. Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s tears had completely discomfited me; I\r\npositively did not know what to think, and was ready to cry myself: I\r\nwas still a child, in spite of my sixteen years. I thought no more of\r\nMal\xc3\xa9vsky, although Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff became more and more menacing every day,\r\nand glared at the shifty Count like a wolf at a sheep; but I was not\r\nthinking of anything or of anybody. I lost myself in conjectures and\r\nkept seeking isolated spots. I took a special fancy to the ruins of the\r\nhothouse. I could clamber up on the high wall, seat myself, and sit\r\nthere such an unhappy, lonely, and sad youth that I felt sorry for\r\nmyself--and how delightful those mournful sensations were, how I gloated\r\nover them!...\r\n\r\nOne day, I was sitting thus on the wall, gazing off into the distance\r\nand listening to the chiming of the bells ... when suddenly something\r\nran over me--not a breeze exactly, not a shiver, but something\r\nresembling a breath, the consciousness of some one\xe2\x80\x99s proximity.... I\r\ndropped my eyes. Below me, in a light grey gown, with a pink parasol on\r\nher shoulder, Zina\xc3\xadda was walking hastily along the road. She saw me,\r\nhalted, and, pushing up the brim of her straw hat, raised her velvety\r\neyes to mine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing there, on such a height?\xe2\x80\x9d--she asked me, with a\r\nstrange sort of smile.--\xe2\x80\x9cThere now,\xe2\x80\x9d--she went on,--\xe2\x80\x9cyou are always\r\ndeclaring that you love me--jump down to me here on the road if you\r\nreally do love me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBefore the words were well out of Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s mouth I had flown down,\r\nexactly as though some one had given me a push from behind. The wall was\r\nabout two fathoms high. I landed on the ground with my feet, but the\r\nshock was so violent that I could not retain my balance; I fell, and\r\nlost consciousness for a moment. When I came to myself I felt, without\r\nopening my eyes, that Zina\xc3\xadda was by my side.--\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear boy,\xe2\x80\x9d--she was\r\nsaying, as she bent over me--and tender anxiety was audible in her\r\nvoice--\xe2\x80\x9chow couldst thou do that, how couldst thou obey?... I love thee\r\n... rise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer breast was heaving beside me, her hands were touching my head, and\r\nsuddenly--what were my sensations then!--her soft, fresh lips began to\r\ncover my whole face with kisses ... they touched my lips.... But at this\r\npoint Zina\xc3\xadda probably divined from the expression of my face that I had\r\nalready recovered consciousness, although I still did not open my\r\neyes--and swiftly rising to her feet, she said:--\xe2\x80\x9cCome, get up, you\r\nrogue, you foolish fellow! Why do you lie there in the dust?\xe2\x80\x9d--I got up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me my parasol,\xe2\x80\x9d--said Zina\xc3\xadda.--\xe2\x80\x9cI have thrown it somewhere; and\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t look at me like that what nonsense is this? You are hurt? You have\r\nburned yourself with the nettles, I suppose. Don\xe2\x80\x99t look at me like that,\r\nI tell you.... Why, he understands nothing, he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t answer me,\xe2\x80\x9d--she\r\nadded, as though speaking to herself.... \xe2\x80\x9cGo home, M\xe2\x80\x99sieu Voldemar,\r\nbrush yourself off, and don\xe2\x80\x99t dare to follow me--if you do I shall be\r\nvery angry, and I shall never again....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not finish her speech and walked briskly away, while I sat down\r\nby the roadside ... my legs would not support me. The nettles had stung\r\nmy hands, my back ached, and my head was reeling; but the sensation of\r\nbeatitude which I then experienced has never since been repeated in my\r\nlife. It hung like a sweet pain in all my limbs and broke out at last in\r\nrapturous leaps and exclamations. As a matter of fact, I was still a\r\nchild.\r\n\r\n\r\nXIII\r\n\r\nI was so happy and proud all that day; I preserved so vividly on my\r\nvisage the feeling of Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s kisses; I recalled her every word with\r\nsuch ecstasy; I so cherished my unexpected happiness that I even became\r\nfrightened; I did not even wish to see her who was the cause of those\r\nnew sensations. It seemed to me that I could ask nothing more of Fate,\r\nthat now I must \xe2\x80\x9ctake and draw a deep breath for the last time, and\r\ndie.\xe2\x80\x9d On the other hand, when I set off for the wing next day, I felt a\r\ngreat agitation, which I vainly endeavoured to conceal beneath the\r\ndiscreet facial ease suitable for a man who wishes to let it be\r\nunderstood that he knows how to keep a secret. Zina\xc3\xadda received me very\r\nsimply, without any emotion, merely shaking her finger at me and asking:\r\nHad I any bruises? All my discreet ease of manner and mysteriousness\r\ninstantly disappeared, and along with them my agitation. Of course I had\r\nnot expected anything in particular, but Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s composure acted on me\r\nlike a dash of cold water. I understood that I was a child in her\r\neyes--and my heart waxed very heavy! Zina\xc3\xadda paced to and fro in the\r\nroom, smiling swiftly every time she glanced at me; but her thoughts\r\nwere far away, I saw that clearly.... \xe2\x80\x9cShall I allude to what happened\r\nyesterday myself,\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought;--\xe2\x80\x9cshall I ask her where she was going in\r\nsuch haste, in order to find out, definitively?\xe2\x80\x9d ... but I merely waved\r\nmy hand in despair and sat down in a corner.\r\n\r\nByelovz\xc3\xb3roff entered; I was delighted to see him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not found you a gentle saddle-horse,\xe2\x80\x9d--he began in a surly\r\ntone;--\xe2\x80\x9cFreitag vouches to me for one--but I am not convinced. I am\r\nafraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf what are you afraid, allow me to inquire?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Zina\xc3\xadda.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf what? Why, you don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to ride. God forbid that any accident\r\nshould happen! And what has put that freak into your head?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, that\xe2\x80\x99s my affair, M\xe2\x80\x99sieu my wild beast. In that case, I will ask\r\nPi\xc3\xb3tr Vas\xc3\xadlievitch\xe2\x80\x9d.... (My father was called Pi\xc3\xb3tr Vas\xc3\xadlievitch.... I\r\nwas amazed that she should mention his name so lightly and freely,\r\nexactly as though she were convinced of his readiness to serve her.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t say so!\xe2\x80\x9d--retorted Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff.--\xe2\x80\x9cIs it with him that you\r\nwish to ride?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith him or some one else,--that makes no difference to you. Only not\r\nwith you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot with me,\xe2\x80\x9d--said Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff.--\xe2\x80\x9cAs you like. What does it matter? I\r\nwill get you the horse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut see to it that it is not a cow-like beast. I warn you in advance\r\nthat I mean to gallop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGallop, if you wish.... But is it with Mal\xc3\xa9vsky that you are going to\r\nride?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I ride with him, warrior? Come, quiet down. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take\r\nyou too. You know that for me Mal\xc3\xa9vsky is now--fie!\xe2\x80\x9d--She shook her\r\nhead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say that just to console me,\xe2\x80\x9d--growled Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff.\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda narrowed her eyes.--\xe2\x80\x9cDoes that console you? oh ... oh oh ...\r\nwarrior!\xe2\x80\x9d--she said at last, as though unable to find any other\r\nword.--\xe2\x80\x9cAnd would you like to ride with us, M\xe2\x80\x99sieu Voldemar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not fond of riding ... in a large party,\xe2\x80\x9d ... I muttered, without\r\nraising my eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou prefer a _t\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-t\xc3\xaate_?... Well, every one to his taste,\xe2\x80\x9d--she\r\nsaid, with a sigh.--\xe2\x80\x9cBut go, Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff, make an effort. I want the\r\nhorse for to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but where am I to get the money?\xe2\x80\x9d--interposed the old Princess.\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda frowned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not asking any from you; Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff will trust me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will, he will,\xe2\x80\x9d grumbled the old Princess--and suddenly screamed at\r\nthe top of her voice:--\xe2\x80\x9cDuny\xc3\xa1shka!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Maman_, I made you a present of a bell,\xe2\x80\x9d--remarked the young Princess.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDuny\xc3\xa1shka!\xe2\x80\x9d--repeated the old woman.\r\n\r\nByelovz\xc3\xb3roff bowed himself out; I went out with him. Zina\xc3\xadda did not\r\ndetain me.\r\n\r\n\r\nXIV\r\n\r\nI rose early the next morning, cut myself a staff, and went off beyond\r\nthe city barrier. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll have a walk and banish my grief,\xe2\x80\x9d--I said to\r\nmyself. It was a beautiful day, brilliant but not too hot; a cheerful,\r\nfresh breeze was blowing over the earth and rustling and playing\r\nmoderately, keeping in constant motion and agitating nothing. For a long\r\ntime I roamed about on the hills and in the forests. I did not feel\r\nhappy; I had left home with the intention of surrendering myself to\r\nmelancholy;--but youth, the fine weather, the fresh air, the diversion\r\nof brisk pedestrian exercise, the delight of lying in solitude on the\r\nthick grass, produced their effect; the memory of those unforgettable\r\nwords, of those kisses, again thrust themselves into my soul. It was\r\npleasant to me to think that Zina\xc3\xadda could not, nevertheless, fail to do\r\njustice to my decision, to my heroism.... \xe2\x80\x9cOthers are better for her\r\nthan I,\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought:--\xe2\x80\x9cso be it! On the other hand, the others only say\r\nwhat they will do, but I have done it! And what else am I capable of\r\ndoing for her?\xe2\x80\x9d--My imagination began to ferment. I began to picture to\r\nmyself how I would save her from the hands of enemies; how, all bathed\r\nin blood, I would wrest her out of prison; how I would die at her feet.\r\nI recalled a picture which hung in our drawing-room of Malek-Adel\r\ncarrying off Matilda--and thereupon became engrossed in the appearance\r\nof a big, speckled woodpecker which was busily ascending the slender\r\ntrunk of a birch-tree, and uneasily peering out from behind it, now on\r\nthe right, now on the left, like a musician from behind the neck of his\r\nbass-viol.\r\n\r\nThen I began to sing: \xe2\x80\x9cNot the white snows,\xe2\x80\x9d--and ran off into the\r\nromance which was well known at that period, \xe2\x80\x9cI will await thee when the\r\nplayful breeze\xe2\x80\x9d; then I began to recite aloud Erm\xc3\xa1k\xe2\x80\x99s invocation to the\r\nstars in Khomyak\xc3\xb3ff\xe2\x80\x99s tragedy; I tried to compose something in a\r\nsentimental vein; I even thought out the line wherewith the whole poem\r\nwas to conclude: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Zina\xc3\xadda! Zina\xc3\xadda!\xe2\x80\x9d--But it came to nothing.\r\nMeanwhile, dinner-time was approaching. I descended into the valley; a\r\nnarrow, sandy path wound through it and led toward the town. I strolled\r\nalong that path.... The dull trampling of horses\xe2\x80\x99 hoofs resounded behind\r\nme. I glanced round, involuntarily came to a standstill and pulled off\r\nmy cap. I beheld my father and Zina\xc3\xadda. They were riding side by side.\r\nMy father was saying something to her, bending his whole body toward\r\nher, and resting his hand on the neck of her horse; he was smiling.\r\nZina\xc3\xadda was listening to him in silence, with her eyes severely downcast\r\nand lips compressed. At first I saw only them; it was not until several\r\nmoments later that Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff made his appearance from round a turn in\r\nthe valley, dressed in hussar uniform with pelisse, and mounted on a\r\nfoam-flecked black horse. The good steed was tossing his head, snorting\r\nand curvetting; the rider was both reining him in and spurring him on.\r\nI stepped aside. My father gathered up his reins and moved away from\r\nZina\xc3\xadda; she slowly raised her eyes to his--and both set off at a\r\ngallop.... Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff dashed headlong after them with clanking sword.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is as red as a crab,\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought,--\xe2\x80\x9cand she.... Why is she so pale?\r\nShe has been riding the whole morning--and yet she is pale?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI redoubled my pace and managed to reach home just before dinner. My\r\nfather was already sitting, re-dressed, well-washed and fresh, beside my\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s arm-chair, and reading aloud to her in his even, sonorous\r\nvoice, the feuilleton of the _Journal des D\xc3\xa9bats_; but my mother was\r\nlistening to him inattentively and, on catching sight of me, inquired\r\nwhere I had been all day, adding, that she did not like to have me\r\nprowling about God only knew where and God only knew with whom. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\nhave been walking alone,\xe2\x80\x9d--I was on the point of replying; but I glanced\r\nat my father and for some reason or other held my peace.\r\n\r\n\r\nXV\r\n\r\nDuring the course of the next five or six days I hardly saw Zina\xc3\xadda; she\r\ngave it out that she was ill, which did not, however, prevent the\r\nhabitual visitors from presenting themselves at the wing--\xe2\x80\x9cto take their\r\nturn in attendance,\xe2\x80\x9d--as they expressed it;--all except Maid\xc3\xa1noff, who\r\nimmediately became dispirited as soon as he had no opportunity to go\r\ninto raptures. Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff sat morosely in a corner, all tightly\r\nbuttoned up and red in the face; on Count Mal\xc3\xa9vsky\xe2\x80\x99s delicate visage\r\nhovered constantly a sort of evil smile; he really had fallen into\r\ndisfavour with Zina\xc3\xadda and listened with particular pains to the old\r\nPrincess, and drove with her to the Governor-General\xe2\x80\x99s in a hired\r\ncarriage. But this trip proved unsuccessful and even resulted in an\r\nunpleasantness for Mal\xc3\xa9vsky: he was reminded of some row with certain\r\nPut\xc3\xa9isk officers, and was compelled, in self-justification, to say that\r\nhe was inexperienced at the time. L\xc3\xbashin came twice a day, but did not\r\nremain long. I was somewhat afraid of him after our last explanation\r\nand, at the same time, I felt a sincere attachment for him. One day he\r\nwent for a stroll with me in the Nesk\xc3\xbatchny Park, was very good-natured\r\nand amiable, imparted to me the names and properties of various plants\r\nand flowers, and suddenly exclaimed--without rhyme or reason, as the\r\nsaying is--as he smote himself on the brow: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I, like a fool, thought\r\nshe was a coquette! Evidently, it is sweet to sacrifice one\xe2\x80\x99s self--for\r\nsome people!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean to say by that?\xe2\x80\x9d--I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to say anything to you,\xe2\x80\x9d--returned L\xc3\xbashin, abruptly.\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda avoided me; my appearance--I could not but perceive the\r\nfact--produced an unpleasant impression on her. She involuntarily turned\r\naway from me ... involuntarily; that was what was bitter, that was what\r\nbroke my heart! But there was no help for it and I tried to keep out of\r\nher sight and only stand guard over her from a distance, in which I was\r\nnot always successful. As before, something incomprehensible was taking\r\nplace with her; her face had become different--she was altogether a\r\ndifferent person. I was particularly struck by the change which had\r\ntaken place in her on a certain warm, tranquil evening. I was sitting on\r\na low bench under a wide-spreading elder-bush; I loved that little nook;\r\nthe window of Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s chamber was visible thence. I was sitting there;\r\nover my head, in the darkened foliage, a tiny bird was rummaging fussily\r\nabout; a great cat with outstretched back had stolen into the garden,\r\nand the first beetles were booming heavily in the air, which was still\r\ntransparent although no longer light. I sat there and stared at the\r\nwindow, and waited to see whether some one would not open it: and, in\r\nfact, it did open, and Zina\xc3\xadda made her appearance in it. She wore a\r\nwhite gown, and she herself--her face, her shoulders and her hands--was\r\npale to whiteness. She remained for a long time motionless, and for a\r\nlong time stared, without moving, straight in front of her from beneath\r\nher contracted brows. I did not recognise that look in her. Then she\r\nclasped her hands very, very tightly, raised them to her lips, to her\r\nforehead--and suddenly, unlocking her fingers, pushed her hair away from\r\nher ears, shook it back and, throwing her head downward from above with\r\na certain decisiveness, she shut the window with a bang.\r\n\r\nTwo days later she met me in the park. I tried to step aside, but she\r\nstopped me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me your hand\xe2\x80\x9d--she said to me, with her former affection.--\xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\na long time since you and I have had a chat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked at her; her eyes were beaming softly and her face was smiling,\r\nas though athwart a mist.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you still ailing?\xe2\x80\x9d--I asked her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, everything has passed off now,\xe2\x80\x9d--she replied, breaking off a small,\r\nred rose.--\xe2\x80\x9cI am a little tired, but that will pass off also.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd will you be once more the same as you used to be?\xe2\x80\x9d--I queried.\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda raised the rose to her face, and it seemed to me as though the\r\nreflection of the brilliant petals fell upon her cheeks.--\xe2\x80\x9cHave I\r\nchanged?\xe2\x80\x9d--she asked me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you have changed,\xe2\x80\x9d--I replied in a low voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was cold toward you,--I know that,\xe2\x80\x9d--began Zina\xc3\xadda;--\xe2\x80\x9cbut you must\r\nnot pay any heed to that.... I could not do otherwise.... Come, what\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe use of talking about that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not want me to love you--that\xe2\x80\x99s what!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed gloomily,\r\nwith involuntary impetuosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, love me, but not as before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us be friends,--that is how!\xe2\x80\x9d--Zina\xc3\xadda allowed me to smell of the\r\nrose.--\xe2\x80\x9cListen; I am much older than you, you know--I might be your\r\naunt, really; well, if not your aunt, then your elder sister. While\r\nyou....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a child to you,\xe2\x80\x9d--I interrupted her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes, you are a child, but a dear, good, clever child, of whom I\r\nam very fond. Do you know what? I will appoint you to the post of my\r\npage from this day forth; and you are not to forget that pages must not\r\nbe separated from their mistress. Here is a token of your new dignity\r\nfor you,\xe2\x80\x9d--she added, sticking the rose into the button-hole of my\r\nround-jacket; \xe2\x80\x9ca token of our favour toward you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have received many favours from you in the past,\xe2\x80\x9d--I murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d--said Zina\xc3\xadda, and darting a sidelong glance at me.--\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a\r\nmemory you have! Well? And I am ready now also....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd bending toward me, she imprinted on my brow a pure, calm kiss.\r\n\r\nI only stared at her--but she turned away and, saying,--\xe2\x80\x9cFollow me, my\r\npage,\xe2\x80\x9d--walked to the wing. I followed her--and was in a constant state\r\nof bewilderment.--\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible,\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought,--\xe2\x80\x9cthat this gentle,\r\nsensible young girl is that same Zina\xc3\xadda whom I used to know?\xe2\x80\x9d--And her\r\nvery walk seemed to me more quiet, her whole figure more majestic, more\r\ngraceful....\r\n\r\nAnd, my God! with what fresh violence did love flame up within me!\r\n\r\n\r\nXVI\r\n\r\nAfter dinner the visitors were assembled again in the wing, and the\r\nyoung Princess came out to them. The whole company was present, in full\r\nforce, as on that first evening, never to be forgotten by me: even\r\nNirm\xc3\xa1tzky had dragged himself thither. Maid\xc3\xa1noff had arrived earlier\r\nthan all the rest; he had brought some new verses. The game of forfeits\r\nbegan again, but this time without the strange sallies, without pranks\r\nand uproar; the gipsy element had vanished. Zina\xc3\xadda gave a new mood to\r\nour gathering. I sat beside her, as a page should. Among other things,\r\nshe proposed that the one whose forfeit was drawn should narrate his\r\ndream; but this was not a success. The dreams turned out to be either\r\nuninteresting (Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff had dreamed that he had fed his horse on\r\ncarp, and that it had a wooden head), or unnatural, fictitious.\r\nMaid\xc3\xa1noff regaled us with a complete novel; there were sepulchres and\r\nangels with harps, and burning lights and sounds wafted from afar.\r\nZina\xc3\xadda did not allow him to finish. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it is a question of\r\ninvention,\xe2\x80\x9d--said she,--\xe2\x80\x9cthen let each one relate something which is\r\npositively made up.\xe2\x80\x9d--Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff had to speak first.\r\n\r\nThe young hussar became confused.--\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot invent anything!\xe2\x80\x9d--he\r\nexclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat nonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d--interposed Zina\xc3\xadda.--\xe2\x80\x9cCome, imagine, for instance,\r\nthat you are married, and tell us how you would pass the time with your\r\nwife. Would you lock her up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd would you sit with her yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly would sit with her myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good. Well, and what if that bored her, and she betrayed you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would kill her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so. Well, now supposing that I were your wife, what would you do\r\nthen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nByelovz\xc3\xb3roff made no answer for a while.--\xe2\x80\x9cI would kill myself....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda burst out laughing.--\xe2\x80\x9cI see that there\xe2\x80\x99s not much to be got out\r\nof you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe second forfeit fell to Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s share. She raised her eyes to the\r\nceiling and meditated.--\xe2\x80\x9cSee here,\xe2\x80\x9d--she began at last,--\xe2\x80\x9cthis is what I\r\nhave devised.... Imagine to yourselves a magnificent palace, a summer\r\nnight, and a marvellous ball. This ball is given by the young Queen.\r\nEverywhere there are gold, marble, silk, lights, diamonds, flowers, the\r\nsmoke of incense--all the whims of luxury.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you love luxury?\xe2\x80\x9d--interrupted L\xc3\xbashin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLuxury is beautiful,\xe2\x80\x9d--she returned;--\xe2\x80\x9cI love everything that is\r\nbeautiful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than what is fine?\xe2\x80\x9d--he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is difficult; somehow I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand. Don\xe2\x80\x99t bother me. So\r\nthen, there is a magnificent ball. There are many guests, they are all\r\nyoung, very handsome, brave; all are desperately in love with the\r\nQueen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre there no women among the guests?\xe2\x80\x9d--inquired Mal\xc3\xa9vsky.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--or stay--yes, there are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlso very handsome?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharming. But the men are all in love with the Queen. She is tall and\r\nslender; she wears a small gold diadem on her black hair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked at Zina\xc3\xadda--and at that moment she seemed so far above us, her\r\nwhite forehead and her impassive eyebrows exhaled so much clear\r\nintelligence and such sovereignty, that I said to myself: \xe2\x80\x9cThou thyself\r\nart that Queen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll throng around her,\xe2\x80\x9d--pursued Zina\xc3\xadda;--\xe2\x80\x9call lavish the most\r\nflattering speeches on her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is she fond of flattery?\xe2\x80\x9d--asked L\xc3\xbashin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow intolerable! He is continually interrupting.... Who does not like\r\nflattery?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne more final question,\xe2\x80\x9d--remarked Mal\xc3\xa9vsky:--\xe2\x80\x9cHas the Queen a\r\nhusband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not thought about that. No, why should she have a husband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d--assented Mal\xc3\xa9vsky;--\xe2\x80\x9cwhy should she have a husband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSilence!\xe2\x80\x9d--exclaimed, in English, Maid\xc3\xa1noff, who spoke French badly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Merci_,\xe2\x80\x9d--said Zina\xc3\xadda to him.--\xe2\x80\x9cSo then, the Queen listens to those\r\nspeeches, listens to the music, but does not look at a single one of the\r\nguests. Six windows are open from top to bottom, from ceiling to floor,\r\nand behind them are the dark sky with great stars and the dark garden\r\nwith huge trees. The Queen gazes into the garden. There, near the trees\r\nis a fountain: it gleams white athwart the gloom--long, as long as a\r\nspectre. The Queen hears the quiet plashing of its waters in the midst\r\nof the conversation and the music. She gazes and thinks: \xe2\x80\x98All of you\r\ngentlemen are noble, clever, wealthy; you are all ready to die at my\r\nfeet, I rule over you; ... but yonder, by the side of the fountain, by\r\nthe side of that plashing water, there is standing and waiting for me\r\nthe man whom I love, who rules over me. He wears no rich garments, nor\r\nprecious jewels; no one knows him; but he is waiting for me, and is\r\nconvinced that I shall come--and I shall come, and there is no power in\r\nexistence which can stop me when I wish to go to him and remain with\r\nhim and lose myself with him yonder, in the gloom of the park, beneath\r\nthe rustling of the trees, beneath the plashing of the fountain....\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda ceased speaking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that an invention?\xe2\x80\x9d--asked Mal\xc3\xa9vsky slyly.\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda did not even glance at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what should we do, gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d--suddenly spoke up L\xc3\xbashin,--\xe2\x80\x9cif we\r\nwere among the guests and knew about that lucky man by the fountain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay, stay,\xe2\x80\x9d--interposed Zina\xc3\xadda:--\xe2\x80\x9cI myself will tell you what each\r\none of you would do. You, Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff, would challenge him to a duel;\r\nyou, Maid\xc3\xa1noff, would write an epigram on him.... But no--you do not\r\nknow how to write epigrams; you would compose a long iambic poem on him,\r\nafter the style of Barbier, and would insert your production in the\r\n_Telegraph_. You, Nirm\xc3\xa1tzky, would borrow from him ... no, you would\r\nlend him money on interest; you, doctor....\xe2\x80\x9d She paused.... \xe2\x80\x9cI really do\r\nnot know about you,--what you would do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn my capacity of Court-physician,\xe2\x80\x9d replied L\xc3\xbashin, \xe2\x80\x9cI would advise the\r\nQueen not to give balls when she did not feel in the mood for\r\nguests....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you would be in the right. And you, Count?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I?\xe2\x80\x9d--repeated Mal\xc3\xa9vsky, with an evil smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you would offer him some poisoned sugar-plums.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMal\xc3\xa9vsky\xe2\x80\x99s face writhed a little and assumed for a moment a Jewish\r\nexpression; but he immediately burst into a guffaw.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs for you, M\xe2\x80\x99sieu Voldemar....\xe2\x80\x9d went on Zina\xc3\xadda,--\xe2\x80\x9cbut enough of this;\r\nlet us play at some other game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cM\xe2\x80\x99sieu Voldemar, in his capacity of page to the Queen, would hold up\r\nher train when she ran off into the park,\xe2\x80\x9d--remarked Mal\xc3\xa9vsky viciously.\r\n\r\nI flared up, but Zina\xc3\xadda swiftly laid her hand on my shoulder and\r\nrising, said in a slightly tremulous voice:--\xe2\x80\x9cI have never given Your\r\nRadiance the right to be insolent, and therefore I beg that you will\r\nwithdraw.\xe2\x80\x9d--She pointed him to the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave mercy, Princess,\xe2\x80\x9d--mumbled Mal\xc3\xa9vsky, turning pale all over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Princess is right,\xe2\x80\x9d--exclaimed Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff, rising to his feet\r\nalso.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy God! I never in the least expected this,\xe2\x80\x9d--went on Mal\xc3\xa9vsky:--\xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthink there was nothing in my words which.... I had no intention of\r\noffending you.... Forgive me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda surveyed him with a cold glance, and smiled coldly.--\xe2\x80\x9cRemain, if\r\nyou like,\xe2\x80\x9d--she said, with a careless wave of her hand.--\xe2\x80\x9cM\xe2\x80\x99sieu\r\nVoldemar and I have taken offence without cause. You find it merry to\r\njest.... I wish you well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForgive me,\xe2\x80\x9d--repeated Mal\xc3\xa9vsky once more; and I, recalling Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmovement, thought again that a real queen could not have ordered an\r\ninsolent man out of the room with more majesty.\r\n\r\nThe game of forfeits did not continue long after this little scene; all\r\nfelt somewhat awkward, not so much in consequence of the scene itself as\r\nfrom another, not entirely defined, but oppressive sensation. No one\r\nalluded to it, but each one was conscious of its existence within\r\nhimself and in his neighbour. Maid\xc3\xa1noff recited to us all his poems--and\r\nMal\xc3\xa9vsky lauded them with exaggerated warmth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow hard he is trying to appear amiable now,\xe2\x80\x9d--L\xc3\xbashin whispered to me.\r\n\r\nWe soon dispersed. Zina\xc3\xadda had suddenly grown pensive; the old Princess\r\nsent word that she had a headache; Nirm\xc3\xa1tzky began to complain of his\r\nrheumatism....\r\n\r\nFor a long time I could not get to sleep; Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s narrative had\r\nimpressed me.--\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible that it contains a hint?\xe2\x80\x9d--I asked\r\nmyself:--\xe2\x80\x9cand at whom was she hinting? And if there really is some one\r\nto hint about ... what must I decide to do? No, no, it cannot be,\xe2\x80\x9d--I\r\nwhispered, turning over from one burning cheek to the other.... But I\r\ncalled to mind the expression of Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s face during her narration....\r\nI called to mind the exclamation which had broken from L\xc3\xbashin in the\r\nNesk\xc3\xbatchny Park, the sudden changes in her treatment of me--and lost\r\nmyself in conjectures. \xe2\x80\x9cWho is he?\xe2\x80\x9d Those three words seemed to stand in\r\nfront of my eyes, outlined in the darkness; a low-lying, ominous cloud\r\nseemed to be hanging over me--and I felt its pressure--and waited every\r\nmoment for it to burst. I had grown used to many things of late; I had\r\nseen many things at the Zasy\xc3\xa9kins\xe2\x80\x99; their disorderliness, tallow\r\ncandle-ends, broken knives and forks, gloomy Vonif\xc3\xa1ty, the shabby maids,\r\nthe manners of the old Princess herself,--all that strange life no\r\nlonger surprised me.... But to that which I now dimly felt in Zina\xc3\xadda I\r\ncould not get used.... \xe2\x80\x9cAn adventuress,\xe2\x80\x9d--my mother had one day said\r\nconcerning her. An adventuress--she, my idol, my divinity! That\r\nappellation seared me; I tried to escape from it by burrowing into my\r\npillow; I raged--and at the same time, to what would not I have agreed,\r\nwhat would not I have given, if only I might be that happy mortal by the\r\nfountain!...\r\n\r\nMy blood grew hot and seethed within me. \xe2\x80\x9cA garden ... a fountain,\xe2\x80\x9d ...\r\nI thought.... \xe2\x80\x9cI will go into the garden.\xe2\x80\x9d I dressed myself quickly and\r\nslipped out of the house. The night was dark, the trees were barely\r\nwhispering; a quiet chill was descending from the sky, an odour of\r\nfennel was wafted from the vegetable-garden. I made the round of all the\r\nalleys; the light sound of my footsteps both disconcerted me and gave me\r\ncourage; I halted, waiting and listening to hear how my heart was\r\nbeating quickly and violently. At last I approached the fence and leaned\r\nagainst a slender post. All at once--or was it only my imagination?--a\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s figure flitted past a few paces distant from me.... I strained\r\nmy eyes intently on the darkness; I held my breath. What was this? Was\r\nit footsteps that I heard or was it the thumping of my heart\r\nagain?--\xe2\x80\x9cWho is here?\xe2\x80\x9d--I stammered in barely audible tones. What was\r\nthat again? A suppressed laugh?... or a rustling in the leaves?... or a\r\nsigh close to my very ear? I was terrified.... \xe2\x80\x9cWho is here?\xe2\x80\x9d--I\r\nrepeated, in a still lower voice.\r\n\r\nThe breeze began to flutter for a moment; a fiery band flashed across\r\nthe sky; a star shot down.--\xe2\x80\x9cIs it Zina\xc3\xadda?\xe2\x80\x9d--I tried to ask, but the\r\nsound died on my lips. And suddenly everything became profoundly silent\r\nall around, as often happens in the middle of the night.... Even the\r\nkatydids ceased to shrill in the trees; only a window rattled somewhere.\r\nI stood and stood, then returned to my chamber, to my cold bed. I felt a\r\nstrange agitation--exactly as though I had gone to a tryst, and had\r\nremained alone, and had passed by some one else\xe2\x80\x99s happiness.\r\n\r\n\r\nXVII\r\n\r\nThe next day I caught only a glimpse of Zina\xc3\xadda; she drove away\r\nsomewhere with the old Princess in a hired carriage. On the other hand,\r\nI saw L\xc3\xbashin--who, however, barely deigned to bestow a greeting on\r\nme--and Mal\xc3\xa9vsky. The young Count grinned and entered into conversation\r\nwith me in friendly wise. Among all the visitors to the wing he alone\r\nhad managed to effect an entrance to our house, and my mother had taken\r\na fancy to him. My father did not favour him and treated him politely to\r\nthe point of insult.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, _monsieur le page_,\xe2\x80\x9d--began Mal\xc3\xa9vsky,--\xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad to meet you.\r\nWhat is your beauteous queen doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis fresh, handsome face was so repulsive to me at that moment, and he\r\nlooked at me with such a scornfully-playful stare, that I made him no\r\nanswer whatsoever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you still in a bad humour?\xe2\x80\x9d--he went on.--\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no occasion for\r\nit. It was not I, you know, who called you a page; and pages are chiefly\r\nwith queens. But permit me to observe to you that you are fulfilling\r\nyour duties badly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPages ought to be inseparable from their sovereigns; pages ought to\r\nknow everything that they do; they ought even to watch over them,\xe2\x80\x9d--he\r\nadded, lowering his voice,--\xe2\x80\x9cday and night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean by that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do I mean? I think I have expressed myself plainly. Day--and\r\nnight. It does not matter so much about the day; by day it is light and\r\nthere are people about; but by night--that\xe2\x80\x99s exactly the time to expect\r\na catastrophe. I advise you not to sleep o\xe2\x80\x99nights and to watch, watch\r\nwith all your might. Remember--in a garden, by night, near the\r\nfountain--that\xe2\x80\x99s where you must keep guard. You will thank me for this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMal\xc3\xa9vsky laughed and turned his back on me. He did not, in all\r\nprobability, attribute any special importance to what he had said to me;\r\nhe bore the reputation of being a capital hand at mystification, and was\r\nrenowned for his cleverness in fooling people at the masquerades, in\r\nwhich that almost unconscious disposition to lie, wherewith his whole\r\nbeing was permeated, greatly aided him.... He had merely wished to tease\r\nme; but every word of his trickled like poison through all my\r\nveins.--The blood flew to my head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! so that\xe2\x80\x99s it!\xe2\x80\x9d--I said to myself:--\xe2\x80\x9cgood! So it was not for nothing\r\nthat I felt drawn to the garden! That shall not be!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed,\r\nsmiting myself on the breast with my fist; although I really did not\r\nknow what it was that I was determined not to permit.--\xe2\x80\x9cWhether Mal\xc3\xa9vsky\r\nhimself comes into the garden,\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought (perhaps he had blurted out a\r\nsecret; he was insolent enough for that),--\xe2\x80\x9cor some one else,\xe2\x80\x9d--(the\r\nfence of our vegetable-garden was very low and it cost no effort to\r\nclimb over it)--\xe2\x80\x9cat any rate, it will be all the worse for the person\r\nwhom I catch! I would not advise any one to encounter me! I\xe2\x80\x99ll show the\r\nwhole world and her, the traitress,\xe2\x80\x9d--(I actually called her a\r\ntraitress)--\xe2\x80\x9cthat I know how to avenge myself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI returned to my own room, took out of my writing-table a recently\r\npurchased English knife, felt of the sharp blade, and, knitting my\r\nbrows, thrust it into my pocket with a cold and concentrated decision,\r\nexactly as though it was nothing remarkable for me to do such deeds, and\r\nthis was not the first occasion. My heart swelled angrily within me and\r\ngrew stony; I did not unbend my brows until nightfall and did not relax\r\nmy lips, and kept striding back and forth, clutching the knife which had\r\ngrown warm in my pocket, and preparing myself in advance for something\r\nterrible. These new, unprecedented emotions so engrossed and even\r\ncheered me, that I thought very little about Zina\xc3\xadda herself. There kept\r\nconstantly flitting through my head Aleko, the young gipsy:[6]--\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\r\nart thou going, handsome youth?--Lie down....\xe2\x80\x9d and then: \xe2\x80\x9cThou\xe2\x80\x99rt all\r\nwith blood bespattered!... Oh, what is\xe2\x80\x99t that thou hast done?...\r\nNothing!\xe2\x80\x9d With what a harsh smile I repeated that: that \xe2\x80\x9cNothing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy father was not at home; but my mother, who for some time past had\r\nbeen in a state of almost constant, dull irritation, noticed my baleful\r\naspect at supper, and said to me:--\xe2\x80\x9cWhat art thou sulking at, like a\r\nmouse at groats?\xe2\x80\x9d--I merely smiled patronisingly at her by way of reply\r\nand thought to myself: \xe2\x80\x9cIf they only knew!\xe2\x80\x9d--The clock struck eleven; I\r\nwent to my own room but did not undress; I was waiting for midnight; at\r\nlast it struck.--\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Tis time!\xe2\x80\x9d--I hissed between my teeth, and buttoning\r\nmy coat to the throat and even turning up my sleeves I betook myself to\r\nthe garden.\r\n\r\nI had selected a place beforehand where I meant to stand on guard. At\r\nthe end of the garden, at the spot where the fence, which separated our\r\nproperty from the Zasy\xc3\xa9kins\xe2\x80\x99, abutted on the party-wall, grew a solitary\r\nspruce-tree. Standing beneath its low, thick branches, I could see well,\r\nas far as the nocturnal gloom permitted, all that went on around; there\r\nalso meandered a path which always seemed to me mysterious; like a\r\nserpent it wound under the fence, which at that point bore traces of\r\nclambering feet, and led to an arbour of dense acacias. I reached the\r\nspruce-tree, leaned against its trunk and began my watch.\r\n\r\nThe night was as tranquil as the preceding one had been; but there were\r\nfewer storm-clouds in the sky, and the outlines of the bushes, even of\r\nthe tall flowers, were more plainly discernible. The first moments of\r\nwaiting were wearisome, almost terrible. I had made up my mind to\r\neverything; I was merely considering how I ought to act. Ought I to\r\nthunder out: \xe2\x80\x9cWho goes there? Halt! Confess--or die!\xe2\x80\x9d--or simply\r\nsmite.... Every sound, every noise and rustling seemed to me\r\nsignificant, unusual.... I made ready.... I bent forward.... But half an\r\nhour, an hour, elapsed; my blood quieted down and turned cold; the\r\nconsciousness that I was doing all this in vain, that I was even\r\nsomewhat ridiculous, that Mal\xc3\xa9vsky had been making fun of me, began to\r\nsteal into my soul. I abandoned my ambush and made the round of the\r\nentire garden. As though expressly, not the slightest sound was to be\r\nheard anywhere; everything was at rest; even our dog was asleep, curled\r\nup in a ball at the gate. I climbed up on the ruin of the hothouse,\r\nbeheld before me the distant plain, recalled my meeting with Zina\xc3\xadda,\r\nand became immersed in meditation....\r\n\r\nI started.... I thought I heard the creak of an opening door, then the\r\nlight crackling of a broken twig. In two bounds I had descended from\r\nthe ruin--and stood petrified on the spot. Swift, light but cautious\r\nfootsteps were plainly audible in the garden. They were coming toward\r\nme. \xe2\x80\x9cHere he is.... Here he is, at last!\xe2\x80\x9d--darted through my heart. I\r\nconvulsively jerked the knife out of my pocket, convulsively opened\r\nit--red sparks whirled before my eyes, the hair stood up on my head with\r\nfright and wrath.... The steps were coming straight toward me--I bent\r\nover, and went to meet them.... A man made his appearance.... My God! It\r\nwas my father!\r\n\r\nI recognised him instantly, although he was all enveloped in a dark\r\ncloak,--and had pulled his hat down over his face. He went past me on\r\ntiptoe. He did not notice me although nothing concealed me; but I had so\r\ncontracted myself and shrunk together that I think I must have been on a\r\nlevel with the ground. The jealous Othello, prepared to murder, had\r\nsuddenly been converted into the school-boy.... I was so frightened by\r\nthe unexpected apparition of my father that I did not even take note, at\r\nfirst, in what direction he was going and where he had disappeared. I\r\nmerely straightened up at the moment and thought: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy is my father\r\nwalking in the garden by night?\xe2\x80\x9d--when everything around had relapsed\r\ninto silence. In my alarm I had dropped my knife in the grass, but I did\r\nnot even try to find it; I felt very much ashamed. I became sobered on\r\nthe instant. But as I wended my way home, I stepped up to my little\r\nbench under the elder-bush and cast a glance at the little window of\r\nZina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s chamber. The small, somewhat curved panes of the little window\r\ngleamed dully blue in the faint light which fell from the night sky.\r\nSuddenly their colour began to undergo a change.... Behind them--I saw\r\nit, saw it clearly,--a whitish shade was lowered, descended to the\r\nsill,--and there remained motionless.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the meaning of that?\xe2\x80\x9d--I said aloud, almost involuntarily, when\r\nI again found myself in my own room.--\xe2\x80\x9cWas it a dream, an accident,\r\nor....\xe2\x80\x9d The surmises which suddenly came into my head were so new and\r\nstrange that I dared not even yield to them.\r\n\r\n\r\nXVIII\r\n\r\nI rose in the morning with a headache. My agitation of the night before\r\nhad vanished. It had been replaced by an oppressive perplexity and a\r\ncertain, hitherto unknown sadness,--exactly as though something had died\r\nin me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes you look like a rabbit which has had half of its brain\r\nremoved?\xe2\x80\x9d--said L\xc3\xbashin, who happened to meet me. At breakfast I kept\r\ncasting covert glances now at my father, now at my mother; he was calm,\r\nas usual; she, as usual, was secretly irritated. I waited to see\r\nwhether my father would address me in a friendly way, as he sometimes\r\ndid.... But he did not even caress me with his cold, everyday\r\naffection.--\xe2\x80\x9cShall I tell Zina\xc3\xadda all?\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought.... \xe2\x80\x9cFor it makes no\r\ndifference now--everything is over between us.\xe2\x80\x9d I went to her, but I not\r\nonly did not tell her anything,--I did not even get a chance to talk to\r\nher as I would have liked. The old Princess\xe2\x80\x99s son, a cadet aged twelve,\r\nhad come from Petersburg to spend his vacation with her; Zina\xc3\xadda\r\nimmediately confided her brother to me.--\xe2\x80\x9cHere, my dear Vol\xc3\xb3dya,\xe2\x80\x9d--said\r\nshe (she called me so for the first time), \xe2\x80\x9cis a comrade for you. His\r\nname is Vol\xc3\xb3dya also. Pray, like him; he\xe2\x80\x99s a wild little fellow still,\r\nbut he has a good heart. Show him Nesk\xc3\xbatchny Park, walk with him, take\r\nhim under your protection. You will do that, will you not? You, too, are\r\nsuch a good fellow!\xe2\x80\x9d--She laid both hands affectionately on my\r\nshoulder--and I was reduced to utter confusion. The arrival of that boy\r\nturned me into a boy. I stared in silence at the cadet, who riveted his\r\neyes in corresponding silence on me. Zina\xc3\xadda burst out laughing and\r\npushed us toward each other.--\xe2\x80\x9cCome, embrace, children!\xe2\x80\x9d--We\r\nembraced.--\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take you into the garden if you wish,--shall I?\xe2\x80\x9d--I\r\nasked the cadet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d--he replied, in a hoarse, genuine cadet voice. Again\r\nZina\xc3\xadda indulged in a burst of laughter.... I managed to notice that\r\nnever before had she had such charming colour in her face. The cadet and\r\nI went off together. In our garden stood an old swing. I seated him on\r\nthe thin little board and began to swing him. He sat motionless in his\r\nnew little uniform of thick cloth with broad gold galloon, and clung\r\ntightly to the ropes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better unhook your collar,\xe2\x80\x9d--I said to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, sir,[7] we are used to it, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d--he said, and cleared his\r\nthroat.\r\n\r\nHe resembled his sister; his eyes were particularly suggestive of her.\r\nIt was pleasant to me to be of service to him; and, at the same time,\r\nthat aching pain kept quietly gnawing at my heart. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I really am a\r\nchild,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought; \xe2\x80\x9cbut last night....\xe2\x80\x9d I remembered where I had dropped\r\nmy knife and found it. The cadet asked me to lend it to him, plucked a\r\nthick stalk of lovage, cut a whistle from it, and began to pipe. Othello\r\npiped also.\r\n\r\nBut in the evening, on the other hand, how he did weep, that same\r\nOthello, over Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s hands when, having sought him out in a corner of\r\nthe garden, she asked him what made him so melancholy. My tears streamed\r\nwith such violence that she was frightened.--\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter with\r\nyou? What is the matter with you, Vol\xc3\xb3dya?\xe2\x80\x9d--she kept repeating, and\r\nseeing that I made her no reply, she took it into her head to kiss my\r\nwet cheek. But I turned away from her and whispered through my sobs:--\xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nknow everything: why have you trifled with me?... Why did you want my\r\nlove?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am to blame toward you, Vol\xc3\xb3dya\xe2\x80\x9d ... said Zina\xc3\xadda.--\xe2\x80\x9cAkh, I am very\r\nmuch to blame\xe2\x80\x9d ... she said, and clenched her hands.--\xe2\x80\x9cHow much evil,\r\ndark, sinful, there is in me!... But I am not trifling with you now, I\r\nlove you--you do not suspect why and how.... But what is it you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat could I say to her? She stood before me and gazed at me--and I\r\nbelonged to her wholly, from head to foot, as soon as she looked at\r\nme.... A quarter of an hour later I was running a race with the cadet\r\nand Zina\xc3\xadda; I was not weeping; I was laughing, although my swollen\r\neyelids dropped tears from laughing; on my neck, in place of a tie, was\r\nbound a ribbon of Zina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s, and I shouted with joy when I succeeded in\r\nseizing her round the waist. She did with me whatsoever she would.\r\n\r\n\r\nXIX\r\n\r\nI should be hard put to it, if I were made to narrate in detail all that\r\nwent on within me in the course of the week which followed my\r\nunsuccessful nocturnal expedition. It was a strange, feverish time, a\r\nsort of chaos in which the most opposite emotions, thoughts, suspicions,\r\nhopes, joys, and sufferings revolved in a whirlwind; I was afraid to\r\nlook into myself, if a sixteen-year-old can look into himself; I was\r\nafraid to account to myself for anything whatsoever; I simply made haste\r\nto live through the day until the evening; on the other hand, at night I\r\nslept ... childish giddiness helped me. I did not want to know whether I\r\nwas beloved, and would not admit to myself that I was not beloved; I\r\nshunned my father--but could not shun Zina\xc3\xadda.... I burned as with fire\r\nin her presence, ... but what was the use of my knowing what sort of\r\nfire it was wherewith I burned and melted--seeing that it was sweet to\r\nme to burn and melt! I surrendered myself entirely to my impressions,\r\nand dealt artfully with myself, turned away from my memories and shut my\r\neyes to that of which I had a presentiment in the future.... This\r\nanguish probably would not have continued long ... a thunder-clap put an\r\ninstantaneous end to everything and hurled me into a new course.\r\n\r\nOn returning home one day to dinner from a rather long walk, I learned\r\nwith surprise that I was to dine alone; that my father had gone away,\r\nwhile my mother was ill, did not wish to dine and had shut herself up in\r\nher bedroom. From the footmen\xe2\x80\x99s faces I divined that something unusual\r\nhad taken place.... I dared not interrogate them, but I had a friend,\r\nthe young butler Phil\xc3\xadpp, who was passionately fond of poetry and an\r\nartist on the guitar; I applied to him. From him I learned that a\r\nfrightful scene had taken place between my father and mother (for in the\r\nmaids\xe2\x80\x99 room everything was audible, to the last word; a great deal had\r\nbeen said in French, but the maid M\xc3\xa1sha had lived for five years with a\r\ndressmaker from Paris and understood it all); that my mother had accused\r\nmy father of infidelity, of being intimate with the young lady our\r\nneighbour; that my father had first defended himself, then had flared up\r\nand in his turn had made some harsh remark \xe2\x80\x9cseemingly about her age,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwhich had set my mother to crying; that my mother had also referred to a\r\nnote of hand, which appeared to have been given to the old Princess, and\r\nexpressed herself very vilely about her, and about the young lady as\r\nwell; and that then my father had threatened her.--\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the whole\r\ntrouble arose,\xe2\x80\x9d--pursued Phil\xc3\xadpp, \xe2\x80\x9cout of an anonymous letter; but who\r\nwrote it no one knows; otherwise there was no reason why this affair\r\nshould have come out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut has there been anything?\xe2\x80\x9d--I enunciated with difficulty, while my\r\nhands and feet turned cold, and something began to quiver in the very\r\ndepths of my breast.\r\n\r\nPhil\xc3\xadpp winked significantly.--\xe2\x80\x9cThere has. You can\xe2\x80\x99t conceal such\r\ndoings, cautious as your papa has been in this case;--still, what\r\npossessed him, for example, to hire a carriage, or to ... for you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nget along without people there also.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI dismissed Phil\xc3\xadpp, and flung myself down on my bed. I did not sob, I\r\ndid not give myself up to despair; I did not ask myself when and how all\r\nthis had taken place; I was not surprised that I had not guessed it\r\nsooner, long before--I did not even murmur against my father.... That\r\nwhich I had learned was beyond my strength; this sudden discovery had\r\ncrushed me.... All was over. All my flowers had been plucked up at one\r\nblow and lay strewn around me, scattered and trampled under foot.\r\n\r\n\r\nXX\r\n\r\nOn the following day my mother announced that she was going to remove to\r\ntown. My father went into her bedroom in the morning and sat there a\r\nlong time alone with her. No one heard what he said to her, but my\r\nmother did not weep any more; she calmed down and asked for something to\r\neat, but did not show herself and did not alter her intention. I\r\nremember that I wandered about all day long, but did not go into the\r\ngarden and did not glance even once at the wing--and in the evening I\r\nwas the witness of an amazing occurrence; my father took Count Mal\xc3\xa9vsky\r\nby the arm and led him out of the hall into the anteroom and, in the\r\npresence of a lackey, said coldly to him: \xe2\x80\x9cSeveral days ago Your\r\nRadiance was shown the door in a certain house. I shall not enter into\r\nexplanations with you now, but I have the honour to inform you that if\r\nyou come to my house again I shall fling you through the window. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike your handwriting.\xe2\x80\x9d The Count bowed, set his teeth, shrank together,\r\nand disappeared.\r\n\r\nPreparations began for removing to town, on the Arb\xc3\xa1t,[8] where our\r\nhouse was situated. Probably my father himself no longer cared to remain\r\nin the villa; but it was evident that he had succeeded in persuading my\r\nmother not to make a row. Everything was done quietly, without haste; my\r\nmother even sent her compliments to the old Princess and expressed her\r\nregret that, owing to ill-health, she would be unable to see her before\r\nher departure. I prowled about like a crazy person, and desired but one\r\nthing,--that everything might come to an end as speedily as possible.\r\nOne thought never quitted my head: how could she, a young girl,--well,\r\nand a princess into the bargain,--bring herself to such a step, knowing\r\nthat my father was not a free man while she had the possibility of\r\nmarrying Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff at least, for example? What had she hoped for?\r\nHow was it that she had not been afraid to ruin her whole\r\nfuture?--\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought,--\xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s what love is,--that is\r\npassion,--that is devotion,\xe2\x80\x9d ... and I recalled L\xc3\xbashin\xe2\x80\x99s words to me:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSelf-sacrifice is sweet--for some people.\xe2\x80\x9d Once I happened to catch\r\nsight of a white spot in one of the windows of the wing.... \xe2\x80\x9cCan that be\r\nZina\xc3\xadda\xe2\x80\x99s face?\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought; ... and it really was her face. I could not\r\nhold out. I could not part from her without bidding her a last farewell.\r\nI seized a convenient moment and betook myself to the wing.\r\n\r\nIn the drawing-room the old Princess received me with her customary,\r\nslovenly-careless greeting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has made your folks uneasy so early, my dear fellow?\xe2\x80\x9d--she said,\r\nstuffing snuff up both her nostrils. I looked at her, and a weight was\r\nremoved from my heart. The word \xe2\x80\x9cnote of hand\xe2\x80\x9d uttered by Phil\xc3\xadpp\r\ntormented me. She suspected nothing ... so it seemed to me then, at\r\nleast. Zina\xc3\xadda made her appearance from the adjoining room in a black\r\ngown, pale, with hair out of curl; she silently took me by the hand and\r\nled me away to her room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard your voice,\xe2\x80\x9d--she began,--\xe2\x80\x9cand came out at once. And did you\r\nfind it so easy to desert us, naughty boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have come to take leave of you, Princess,\xe2\x80\x9d--I replied,--\xe2\x80\x9cprobably\r\nforever. You may have heard we are going away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nZina\xc3\xadda gazed intently at me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have heard. Thank you for coming. I was beginning to think that\r\nI should not see you.--Think kindly of me. I have sometimes tormented\r\nyou; but nevertheless I am not the sort of person you think I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned away and leaned against the window-casing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, I am not that sort of person. I know that you have a bad\r\nopinion of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you ... you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI?\xe2\x80\x9d--I repeated sorrowfully, and my heart began to quiver as of old,\r\nbeneath the influence of the irresistible, inexpressible witchery.--\xe2\x80\x9cI?\r\nBelieve me, Zina\xc3\xadda Alex\xc3\xa1ndrovna, whatever you may have done, however\r\nyou may have tormented me, I shall love and adore you until the end of\r\nmy life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe turned swiftly toward me and opening her arms widely, she clasped my\r\nhead, and kissed me heartily and warmly. God knows whom that long,\r\nfarewell kiss was seeking, but I eagerly tasted its sweetness. I knew\r\nthat it would never more be repeated.--\xe2\x80\x9cFarewell, farewell!\xe2\x80\x9d I kept\r\nsaying....\r\n\r\nShe wrenched herself away and left the room. And I withdrew also. I am\r\nunable to describe the feeling with which I retired. I should not wish\r\never to have it repeated; but I should consider myself unhappy if I had\r\nnever experienced it.\r\n\r\nWe removed to town. I did not speedily detach myself from the past, I\r\ndid not speedily take up my work. My wound healed slowly; but I really\r\nhad no evil feeling toward my father. On the contrary, he seemed to have\r\ngained in stature in my eyes ... let the psychologists explain this\r\ncontradiction as best they may. One day I was walking along the\r\nboulevard when, to my indescribable joy, I encountered L\xc3\xbashin. I liked\r\nhim for his straightforward, sincere character; and, moreover, he was\r\ndear to me in virtue of the memories which he awakened in me. I rushed\r\nat him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAha!\xe2\x80\x9d--he said, with a scowl.--\xe2\x80\x9cIs it you, young man? Come, let me have\r\na look at you. You are still all sallow, and yet there is not the olden\r\ntrash in your eyes. You look like a man, not like a lap-dog. That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngood. Well, and how are you? Are you working?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI heaved a sigh. I did not wish to lie, and I was ashamed to tell the\r\ntruth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, never mind,\xe2\x80\x9d--went on L\xc3\xbashin,--\xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid. The principal\r\nthing is to live in normal fashion and not to yield to impulses.\r\nOtherwise, where\xe2\x80\x99s the good? No matter whither the wave bears one--\xe2\x80\x99tis\r\nbad; let a man stand on a stone if need be, but on his own feet. Here I\r\nam croaking ... but Byelovz\xc3\xb3roff--have you heard about him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about him? No.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has disappeared without leaving a trace; they say he has gone to the\r\nCaucasus. A lesson to you, young man. And the whole thing arises from\r\nnot knowing how to say good-bye,--to break bonds in time. You, now, seem\r\nto have jumped out successfully. Look out, don\xe2\x80\x99t fall in again.\r\nFarewell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall not fall in,\xe2\x80\x9d--I thought.... \xe2\x80\x9cI shall see her no more.\xe2\x80\x9d But I\r\nwas fated to see Zina\xc3\xadda once more.\r\n\r\n\r\nXXI\r\n\r\nMy father was in the habit of riding on horseback every day; he had a\r\nsplendid red-roan English horse, with a long, slender neck and long\r\nlegs, indefatigable and vicious. Its name was Electric. No one could\r\nride it except my father. One day he came to me in a kindly frame of\r\nmind, which had not happened with him for a long time: he was preparing\r\nto ride, and had donned his spurs. I began to entreat him to take me\r\nwith him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us, rather, play at leap-frog,\xe2\x80\x9d--replied my father,--\xe2\x80\x9cfor thou wilt\r\nnot be able to keep up with me on thy cob.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I shall; I will put on spurs also.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, come along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe set out.
