Not long before the date of our story, \xe2\x80\x9cthe good-for-nothing\r\nlittle Order of St. Stanislas had been stuck on him,\xe2\x80\x9d as those who\r\nenvied him expressed it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is perfectly just,\xe2\x80\x9d--remarked Skvor\xc3\xa9vitch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one will dispute that,\xe2\x80\x9d--added Kinar\xc3\xa9vitch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI assent also,\xe2\x80\x9d--chimed in, in falsetto, from a corner the master of\r\nthe house, Mr. Finopl\xc3\xa9ntoff.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I, I must confess, cannot assent, because something supernatural\r\nhas happened to me,\xe2\x80\x9d--said a man of medium stature and middle age, with\r\na protruding abdomen and a bald spot, who had been sitting silent before\r\nthe stove up to that moment. The glances of all present in the room were\r\nturned upon him with curiosity and surprise--and silence reigned.\r\n\r\nThis man was a landed proprietor of Kal\xc3\xbaga, not wealthy, who had\r\nrecently come to Petersburg. He had once served in the hussars, had\r\ngambled away his property, resigned from the service and settled down in\r\nthe country. The recent agricultural changes had cut off his revenues,\r\nand he had betaken himself to the capital in search of a snug little\r\nposition. He possessed no abilities, and had no influential connections;\r\nbut he placed great reliance on the friendship of an old comrade in the\r\nservice, who had suddenly, without rhyme or reason, become a person of\r\nimportance, and whom he had once aided to administer a sound thrashing\r\nto a card-sharper. Over and above that he counted upon his own luck--and\r\nit had not betrayed him; several days later he obtained the post of\r\ninspector of government storehouses, a profitable, even honourable\r\nposition which did not require extraordinary talents: the storehouses\r\nthemselves existed only in contemplation, and no one even knew with\r\ncertainty what they were to contain,--but they had been devised as a\r\nmeasure of governmental economy.\r\n\r\nAnt\xc3\xb3n Step\xc3\xa1nitch was the first to break the general silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, my dear sir?\xe2\x80\x9d--he began. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you seriously assert that something\r\nsupernatural--I mean to say, incompatible with the laws of nature--has\r\nhappened to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do,\xe2\x80\x9d--returned \xe2\x80\x9cmy dear sir,\xe2\x80\x9d whose real name was Porf\xc3\xadry\r\nKap\xc3\xadtonitch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIncompatible with the laws of nature?\xe2\x80\x9d--energetically repeated Ant\xc3\xb3n\r\nStep\xc3\xa1nitch, who evidently liked that phrase.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely ... yes; precisely the sort of thing you allude to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is astonishing! What think you, gentlemen?\xe2\x80\x9d--Ant\xc3\xb3n Step\xc3\xa1nitch\r\nendeavoured to impart to his features an ironical expression, but\r\nwithout result--or, to speak more accurately, the only result was to\r\nproduce the effect that Mr. State Councillor smelt a bad odour.--\xe2\x80\x9cWill\r\nnot you be so kind, my dear sir,\xe2\x80\x9d--he went on, addressing the landed\r\nproprietor from Kal\xc3\xbaga,--\xe2\x80\x9cas to communicate to us the particulars of\r\nsuch a curious event?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not? Certainly!\xe2\x80\x9d--replied the landed proprietor, and moving forward\r\nto the middle of the room in an easy manner he spoke as follows:\r\n\r\nI have, gentlemen, as you are probably aware,--or as you may not be\r\naware,--a small estate in Kozy\xc3\xb3l County. I formerly derived some profit\r\nfrom it--but now, of course, nothing but unpleasantness is to be\r\nanticipated. However, let us put politics aside! Well, sir, on that same\r\nestate I have a \xe2\x80\x9cwee little\xe2\x80\x9d manor: a vegetable garden, as is proper, a\r\ntiny pond with little carp, and some sort of buildings--well, and a\r\nsmall wing for my own sinful body.... I am a bachelor. So, sir, one\r\nday--about six years ago--I had returned home rather late; I had been\r\nplaying cards at a neighbour\xe2\x80\x99s house--but I beg you to observe, I was\r\nnot tipsy, as the expression goes. I undressed, got into bed, and blew\r\nout the light. And just imagine, gentlemen; no sooner had I blown out\r\nthe light, than something began to rummage under my bed! Is it a rat? I\r\nthought. No, it was not a rat: it clawed and fidgeted and scratched\r\nitself.... At last it began to flap its ears!\r\n\r\nIt was a dog--that was clear. But where had the dog come from? I keep\r\nnone myself. \xe2\x80\x9cCan some stray animal have run in?\xe2\x80\x9d I thought. I called to\r\nmy servant; his name is F\xc3\xadlka. The man entered with a candle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s this,\xe2\x80\x9d--says I,--\xe2\x80\x9cmy good F\xc3\xadlka? How lax thou art! A dog has\r\nintruded himself under my bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dog?\xe2\x80\x9d--says he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow should I know?\xe2\x80\x9d--says I;--\xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s thy affair--not to allow thy\r\nmaster to be disturbed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy F\xc3\xadlka bent down, and began to pass the candle about under the bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d--says he,--\xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s no dog here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI bent down also; in fact there was no dog.... Here was a marvel! I\r\nturned my eyes on F\xc3\xadlka: he was smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFool,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I to him,--\xe2\x80\x9cwhat art thou grinning about? When thou didst\r\nopen the door the dog probably took and sneaked out into the anteroom.\r\nBut thou, gaper, didst notice nothing, because thou art eternally\r\nasleep. Can it be that thou thinkest I am drunk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe attempted to reply, but I drove him out, curled myself up in a ring,\r\nand heard nothing more that night.\r\n\r\nBut on the following night--just imagine!--the same thing was repeated.\r\nNo sooner had I blown out the light than it began to claw and flap its\r\nears. Again I summoned F\xc3\xadlka, again he looked under the bed--again\r\nnothing! I sent him away, blew out the light--phew, damn it! there was\r\nthe dog still. And a dog it certainly was: I could hear it breathing and\r\nrummaging in its hair with its teeth in search of fleas so plainly!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cF\xc3\xadlka!\xe2\x80\x9d--says I,--\xe2\x80\x9ccome hither without a light!\xe2\x80\x9d... He entered....\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now,\xe2\x80\x9d--says I, \xe2\x80\x9cdost thou hear?...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do,\xe2\x80\x9d--said he. I could not see him, but I felt that the fellow was\r\nquailing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou make of it?\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou command me to make of it, Porf\xc3\xadry Kap\xc3\xadtonitch?... \xe2\x80\x99Tis\r\nan instigation of the Evil One!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art a lewd fellow; hold thy tongue with thy instigation of the\r\nEvil One.\xe2\x80\x9d... But the voices of both of us were like those of birds,\r\nand we were shaking as though in a fever--in the darkness. I lighted a\r\ncandle: there was no dog, and no noise whatever--only F\xc3\xadlka and I as\r\nwhite as clay. And I must inform you, gentlemen--you can believe me or\r\nnot--but from that night forth for the space of six weeks the same thing\r\nwent on. At last I even got accustomed to it and took to extinguishing\r\nmy light because I cannot sleep with a light. \xe2\x80\x9cLet him fidget!\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nthought. \xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t harm me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut--I see--that you do not belong to the cowardly squad,\xe2\x80\x9d--interrupted\r\nAnt\xc3\xb3n Step\xc3\xa1nitch, with a half-scornful, half-condescending laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nhussar is immediately perceptible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should not be frightened at you, in any case,\xe2\x80\x9d--said Porf\xc3\xadry\r\nKap\xc3\xadtonitch, and for a moment he really did look like a hussar.--\xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nlisten further.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA neighbour came to me, the same one with whom I was in the habit of\r\nplaying cards. He dined with me on what God had sent, and lost fifty\r\nrubles to me for his visit; night was drawing on--it was time for him to\r\ngo. But I had calculations of my own:--\xe2\x80\x9cStop and spend the night with\r\nme, Vas\xc3\xadly Vas\xc3\xadlitch; to-morrow thou wilt win it back, God willing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy Vas\xc3\xadly Vas\xc3\xadlitch pondered and pondered--and stayed. I ordered a bed\r\nto be placed for him in my own chamber.... Well, sir, we went to bed,\r\nsmoked, chattered,--chiefly about the feminine sex, as is fitting in\r\nbachelor society,--and laughed, as a matter of course. I look; Vas\xc3\xadly\r\nVas\xc3\xadlitch has put out his candle and has turned his back on me; that\r\nsignifies: \xe2\x80\x9c_Schlafen Sie wohl._\xe2\x80\x9d I waited a little and extinguished my\r\ncandle also. And imagine: before I had time to think to myself, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nsort of performance will there be now?\xe2\x80\x9d my dear little animal began to\r\nmake a row. And that was not all; he crawled out from under the bed,\r\nwalked across the room, clattering his claws on the floor, waggling his\r\nears, and suddenly collided with a chair which stood by the side of\r\nVas\xc3\xadly Vas\xc3\xadlitch\xe2\x80\x99s bed!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPorf\xc3\xadry Kap\xc3\xadtonitch,\xe2\x80\x9d--says Vas\xc3\xadly Vas\xc3\xadlitch, and in such an\r\nindifferent voice, you know,--\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know that thou hadst taken to\r\nkeeping a dog. What sort of an animal is it--a setter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no dog,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I,--\xe2\x80\x9cand I never have had one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast not indeed! But what\xe2\x80\x99s this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this?\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.--\xe2\x80\x9cSee here now; light the candle and thou wilt\r\nfind out for thyself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t a dog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVas\xc3\xadly Vas\xc3\xadlitch turned over in bed.--\xe2\x80\x9cBut thou art jesting, damn it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m not jesting.\xe2\x80\x9d--I hear him go scratch, scratch with a match, and\r\nthat thing does not stop, but scratches its side. The flame flashed up\r\n... and basta! There was not a trace of a dog! Vas\xc3\xadly Vas\xc3\xadlitch stared\r\nat me--and I stared at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of a trick is this?\xe2\x80\x9d--said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I,--\xe2\x80\x9cthis is such a trick that if thou wert to set Socrates\r\nhimself on one side and Frederick the Great on the other even they\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make head or tail of it.\xe2\x80\x9d--And thereupon I told him all in\r\ndetail. Up jumped my Vas\xc3\xadly Vas\xc3\xadlitch as though he had been singed! He\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t get into his boots.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHorses!\xe2\x80\x9d--he yelled--\xe2\x80\x9chorses!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI began to argue with him, but in vain. He simply groaned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t stay,\xe2\x80\x9d--he shouted,--\xe2\x80\x9cnot a minute!--Of course, after this,\r\nthou art a doomed man!--Horses!...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut I prevailed upon him. Only his bed was dragged out into another\r\nroom--and night-lights were lighted everywhere. In the morning, at tea,\r\nhe recovered his dignity; he began to give me advice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou shouldst try absenting thyself from the house for several days,\r\nPorf\xc3\xadry Kap\xc3\xadtonitch,\xe2\x80\x9d he said: \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps that vile thing would leave\r\nthee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut I must tell you that he--that neighbour of mine--had a capacious\r\nmind! he worked his mother-in-law so famously among other things: he\r\npalmed off a note of hand on her; which signifies that he chose the most\r\nvulnerable moment! She became like silk: she gave him a power of\r\nattorney over all her property--what more would you have? But that was a\r\ngreat affair--to twist his mother-in-law round his finger--wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it,\r\nhey? Judge for yourselves. But he went away from me somewhat\r\ndiscontented; I had punished him to the extent of another hundred\r\nrubles. He even swore at me: \xe2\x80\x9cThou art ungrateful,\xe2\x80\x9d--he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthou hast\r\nno feeling;\xe2\x80\x9d but how was I to blame for that? Well, this is in\r\nparenthesis--but I took his suggestion under consideration. That same\r\nday I drove off to town and established myself in an inn, with an\r\nacquaintance, an old man of the Old Ritualist sect.[36]\r\n\r\nHe was a worthy old man, although a trifle harsh, because of\r\nloneliness: his whole family were dead. Only he did not favour tobacco\r\nat all,[37] and felt a great loathing for dogs; I believe, for example,\r\nthat rather than admit a dog into the room he would have rent himself in\r\ntwain! \xe2\x80\x9cFor how is it possible?\xe2\x80\x9d--he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere in my room, on the\r\nwall, the Sovereign Lady herself deigns to dwell;[38] and shall a filthy\r\ndog thrust his accursed snout in there?\xe2\x80\x9d--That was ignorance, of course!\r\nHowever, this is my opinion: if any man has been vouchsafed wisdom, let\r\nhim hold to it!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you are a great philosopher, I see,\xe2\x80\x9d--interrupted Ant\xc3\xb3n Step\xc3\xa1nitch\r\nagain, with the same laugh as before.\r\n\r\nThis time Porf\xc3\xadry Kap\xc3\xadtonitch even scowled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of a philosopher I am no one knows,\xe2\x80\x9d--he said as his\r\nmoustache twitched in a surly manner:--\xe2\x80\x9cbut I would gladly take you as a\r\npupil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all fairly bored our eyes into Ant\xc3\xb3n Step\xc3\xa1nitch; each one of us\r\nexpected an arrogant retort or at least a lightning glance.... But Mr.\r\nState Councillor altered his smile from scorn to indifference, then\r\nyawned, dangled his foot--and that was all!\r\n\r\nSo then, I settled down at that old man\xe2\x80\x99s house--[went on Porf\xc3\xadry\r\nKap\xc3\xadtonitch].--He assigned me a room \xe2\x80\x9cfor acquaintance\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x9d sake,--not of\r\nthe best; he himself lodged there also, behind a partition--and that was\r\nall I required. But what tortures I did undergo! The chamber was small,\r\nit was hot, stifling, and there were flies, and such sticky ones; in the\r\ncorner was a remarkably large case for images, with ancient holy\r\npictures; their garments were dim and puffed out; the air was fairly\r\ninfected with olive-oil, and some sort of a spice in addition; on the\r\nbedstead were two down beds; if you moved a pillow, out ran a cockroach\r\nfrom beneath it.... I drank an incredible amount of tea, out of sheer\r\ntedium--it was simply horrible! I got into bed; it was impossible to\r\nsleep.--And on the other side of the partition my host was sighing and\r\ngrunting and reciting his prayers. I heard him begin to snore--and very\r\nlightly and courteously, in old-fashioned style. I had long since\r\nextinguished my candle--only the shrine-lamp was twinkling in front of\r\nthe holy pictures.... A hindrance, of course! So I took and rose up\r\nsoftly, in my bare feet: I reached up to the lamp and blew it out....\r\nNothing happened.--\xe2\x80\x9cAha!\xe2\x80\x9d I thought: \xe2\x80\x9cthis means that he won\xe2\x80\x99t make a\r\nfuss in the house of strangers.\xe2\x80\x9d... But no sooner had I lain down on\r\nthe bed than the row began again! The thing clawed, and scratched\r\nhimself and flapped his ears ... well, just as I wanted him to. Good! I\r\nlay there and waited to see what would happen. I heard the old man wake\r\nup.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaster,\xe2\x80\x9d--said he,--\xe2\x80\x9chey there, master?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s wanted?\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it thou who didst put out the shrine-lamp?\xe2\x80\x9d--And without awaiting\r\nmy reply, he suddenly began to mumble:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that? What\xe2\x80\x99s that? A dog? A dog? Akh, thou damned Nikonian!\xe2\x80\x9d[39]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit, old man,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I,--\xe2\x80\x9cbefore thou cursest; but it would be\r\nbetter for thee to come hither thyself. Things deserving of wonder are\r\ngoing on here,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.\r\n\r\nThe old man fussed about behind the partition and entered my room with a\r\ncandle, a slender one, of yellow wax; and I was amazed as I looked at\r\nhim! He was all bristling, with shaggy ears and vicious eyes like those\r\nof a polecat; on his head was a small skull-cap of white felt; his beard\r\nreached to his girdle and was white also; and he had on a waistcoat with\r\nbrass buttons over his shirt, and fur boots on his feet, and he\r\ndisseminated an odour of juniper. In that condition he went up to the\r\nholy pictures, crossed himself thrice with two fingers[40] lighted the\r\nshrine-lamp, crossed himself again, and turning to me, merely grunted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExplain thyself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThereupon, without the least delay, I communicated to him all the\r\ncircumstances. The old man listened to all my explanations without\r\nuttering the smallest word; he simply kept shaking his head. Then he sat\r\ndown on my bed, still maintaining silence. He scratched his breast, the\r\nback of his head, and other places, and still remained silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Feod\xc3\xbal Iv\xc3\xa1nitch,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is thy opinion: is this some\r\nsort of visitation of the Evil One, thinkest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man stared at me.--\xe2\x80\x9cA pretty thing thou hast invented! A\r\nvisitation of the Evil One, forsooth! \xe2\x80\x99Twould be all right at thy house,\r\nthou tobacco-user,--but \xe2\x80\x99tis quite another thing here! Only consider how\r\nmany holy things there are here! And thou must needs have a visitation\r\nof the devil!--And if it isn\xe2\x80\x99t that, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man relapsed into silence, scratched himself again, and at last\r\nhe said, but in a dull sort of way, because his moustache kept crawling\r\ninto his mouth:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo thou to the town of By\xc3\xa9leff. There is only one man who can help\r\nthee. And that man dwells in By\xc3\xa9leff;[41] he is one of our people. If\r\nhe takes a fancy to help thee, that\xe2\x80\x99s thy good luck; if he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t take\r\na fancy,--so it must remain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how am I to find him?\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can give thee directions,\xe2\x80\x9d--said he;--\xe2\x80\x9conly why dost thou call this\r\na visitation of the devil? \xe2\x80\x99Tis a vision, or a sign; but thou wilt not\r\nbe able to comprehend it; \xe2\x80\x99tis not within thy flight. And now lie down\r\nand sleep under Christ\xe2\x80\x99s protection, dear little father; I will fumigate\r\nwith incense; and in the morning we will take counsel together. The\r\nmorning is wiser than the evening, thou knowest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, sir, and we did take counsel together in the morning--only I came\r\nnear choking to death with that same incense. And the old man instructed\r\nme after this wise: that when I had reached By\xc3\xa9leff I was to go to the\r\npublic square, and in the second shop on the right inquire for a certain\r\nProkh\xc3\xb3ritch; and having found Prokh\xc3\xb3ritch, I was to hand him a document.\r\nAnd the whole document consisted of a scrap of paper, on which was\r\nwritten the following: \xe2\x80\x9cIn the name of the Father, and of the Son, and\r\nof the Holy Spirit, Amen. To Sergy\xc3\xa9i Prokh\xc3\xb3ritch Perv\xc3\xbashin. Trust this\r\nman. Feod\xc3\xbaly Iv\xc3\xa1novitch.\xe2\x80\x9d And below: \xe2\x80\x9cSend some cabbages, for God\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thanked the old man, and without further ado ordered my tarant\xc3\xa1s to be\r\nharnessed, and set off for By\xc3\xa9leff. For I argued in this way: admitting\r\nthat my nocturnal visitor did not cause me much grief, still,\r\nnevertheless, it was not quite decorous for a nobleman and an\r\nofficer--what do you think about it?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd did you really go to By\xc3\xa9leff?\xe2\x80\x9d--whispered Mr. Finopl\xc3\xa9ntoff.\r\n\r\nI did, straight to By\xc3\xa9leff. I went to the square, and inquired in the\r\nsecond shop on the right for Prokh\xc3\xb3ritch. \xe2\x80\x9cIs there such a man?\xe2\x80\x9d--I\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is,\xe2\x80\x9d--I was told.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd where does he live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the Ok\xc3\xa1, beyond the vegetable-gardens.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn whose house?\xe2\x80\x9d[42]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI wended my way to the Ok\xc3\xa1, searched out his house, that is to say, not\r\nactually a house, but a downright hovel. I beheld a man in a patched\r\nblue overcoat and a tattered cap,--of the petty burgher class, judging\r\nby his appearance,--standing with his back to me, and digging in his\r\ncabbage-garden.--I went up to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you such and such a one?\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.\r\n\r\nHe turned round,--and to tell you the truth, such piercing eyes I have\r\nnever seen in all my life. But his whole face was no bigger than one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfist; his beard was wedge-shaped, and his lips were sunken: he was an\r\naged man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am he,\xe2\x80\x9d--he said.--\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you wanta?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, here,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I;--\xe2\x80\x9cthis is what I wanta,\xe2\x80\x9d--and I placed the\r\ndocument in his hand. He gazed at me very intently, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease come into the house; I cannot read without my spectacles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, sir, he and I went into his kennel--actually, a regular kennel;\r\npoor, bare, crooked; it barely held together. On the wall was a holy\r\npicture of ancient work,[43] as black as a coal; only the whites of the\r\neyes were fairly burning in the faces of the holy people. He took some\r\nround iron spectacles from a small table, placed them on his nose,\r\nperused the writing, and through his spectacles again scrutinised me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have need of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I,--\xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s the fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d--said he, \xe2\x80\x9cif you have, then make your statement, and I will\r\nlisten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd just imagine; he sat down, and pulling a checked handkerchief from\r\nhis pocket, he spread it out on his knees--and the handkerchief was full\r\nof holes--and gazed at me as solemnly as though he had been a\r\nsenator,[44] or some minister or other; and did not ask me to sit down.\r\nAnd what was still more astonishing, I suddenly felt myself growing\r\ntimid, so timid ... simply, my soul sank into my heels. He pierced me\r\nthrough and through with his eyes, and that\xe2\x80\x99s all there is to be said!\r\nBut I recovered my self-possession, and narrated to him my whole story.\r\nHe remained silent for a while, shrank together, mowed with his lips,\r\nand then began to interrogate me, still as though he were a senator, so\r\nmajestically and without haste. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your name?\xe2\x80\x9d--he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cHow old\r\nare you? Who were your parents? Are you a bachelor or married?\xe2\x80\x9d--Then he\r\nbegan to mow with his lips again, frowned, thrust out his finger and\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo reverence to the holy image of the honourable saints of\r\nSolov\xc3\xa9tzk,[45] Z\xc3\xb3sim and Sav\xc3\xa1ty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI made a reverence to the earth, and did not rise to my feet; such awe\r\nand submission did I feel for that man that I believe I would have\r\ninstantly done anything whatsoever he might have ordered me!... I see\r\nthat you are smiling, gentlemen; but I was in no mood for laughing then,\r\nby Heaven I was not.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRise, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d--he said at last.--\xe2\x80\x9cIt is possible to help you. This has\r\nnot been sent to you by way of punishment, but as a warning; it\r\nsignifies that you are being looked after; some one is praying earnestly\r\nfor you. Go now to the bazaar and buy yourself a bitch, which you must\r\nkeep by you day and night, without ceasing. Your visions will cease, and\r\nyour dog will prove necessary to you into the bargain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA flash of light seemed suddenly to illuminate me; how those words did\r\nplease me! I made obeisance to Prokh\xc3\xb3ritch, and was on the point of\r\ndeparting, but remembered that it was impossible for me not to show him\r\nmy gratitude; I drew a three-ruble note from my pocket. But he put aside\r\nmy hand and said to me:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive it to our chapel, or to the poor, for this service is gratis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain I made him an obeisance, nearly to the girdle, and immediately\r\nmarched off to the bazaar. And fancy, no sooner had I begun to approach\r\nthe shops when behold, a man in a frieze cloak advanced to meet me, and\r\nunder his arm he carried a setter bitch, two months old, with\r\nlight-brown hair, a white muzzle, and white fore paws.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalt!\xe2\x80\x9d said I to the man in the frieze cloak; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat will you take for\r\nher?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo rubles in silver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake three!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man was astonished, and thought the gentleman had lost his mind--but\r\nI threw a banknote in his teeth, seized the bitch in my arms, and\r\nrushed to my tarant\xc3\xa1s. The coachman harnessed up the horses briskly, and\r\nthat same evening I was at home. The dog sat on my lap during the whole\r\njourney--and never uttered a sound; but I kept saying to her:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTres\xc3\xb3rushko! Tres\xc3\xb3rushko!\xe2\x80\x9d I immediately gave her food and water,\r\nordered straw to be brought, put her to bed, and dashed into bed myself.\r\nI blew out the light; darkness reigned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome now, begin!\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.--Silence.--\xe2\x80\x9cDo begin, thou thus and\r\nso!\xe2\x80\x9d--Not a sound. It was laughable. I began to take courage.--\xe2\x80\x9cCome\r\nnow, begin, thou thus and so, and \xe2\x80\x99tother thing!\xe2\x80\x9d But nothing\r\nhappened--there was a complete lull! The only thing to be heard was the\r\nbitch breathing hard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cF\xc3\xadlka!\xe2\x80\x9d--I shouted;--\xe2\x80\x9cF\xc3\xadlka! Come hither, stupid man!\xe2\x80\x9d--He\r\nentered.--\xe2\x80\x9cDost thou hear the dog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, master,\xe2\x80\x9d--said he,--\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t hear anything,\xe2\x80\x9d--and began to laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd thou wilt not hear it again forever! Here\xe2\x80\x99s half a ruble for thee\r\nfor vodka!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease let me kiss your hand,\xe2\x80\x9d--said the fool, and crawled to me in the\r\ndark.... My joy was great, I can tell you!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd was that the end of it all?\xe2\x80\x9d--asked Ant\xc3\xb3n Step\xc3\xa1nitch, no longer\r\nironically.\r\n\r\nThe visions did cease, it is true--and there were no disturbances of any\r\nsort--but wait, that was not the end of the whole matter. My\r\nTres\xc3\xb3rushko began to grow, and turned out a cunning rogue. Thick-tailed,\r\nheavy, flop-eared, with drooping dewlaps, she was a regular\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ctake-advance,\xe2\x80\x9d--a thoroughgoing good setter. And moreover, she became\r\ngreatly attached to me. Hunting is bad in our parts,--well, but as I had\r\nset up a dog I had to supply myself with a gun also. I began to roam\r\nabout the surrounding country with my Tres\xc3\xb3r; sometimes I would knock\r\nover a hare (my heavens, how she did course those hares!), and sometimes\r\na quail or a duck. But the chief point was that Tres\xc3\xb3r never, never\r\nstrayed a step away from me. Wherever I went, there she went also; I\r\neven took her to the bath with me--truly! One of our young gentlewomen\r\nundertook to eject me from her drawing-room on account of Tres\xc3\xb3r; but I\r\nraised such a row that I smashed some of her window-panes!\r\n\r\nWell, sir, one day--it happened in summer.... And I must tell you that\r\nthere was such a drought that no one could recall its like; the air was\r\nfull of something which was neither smoke nor fog; there was an odour of\r\nburning, and mist, and the sun was like a red-hot cannon-ball; and the\r\ndust was such that one could not leave off sneezing! People went about\r\nwith their mouths gaping open, just like crows.\r\n\r\nIt bored me to sit at home constantly in complete undress, behind closed\r\nshutters; and by the way, the heat was beginning to moderate.... And\r\nso, gentlemen, I set off afoot to the house of one of my neighbours.\r\nThis neighbour of mine lived about a verst from me,--and was really a\r\nbenevolent lady. She was still young and blooming, and of the most\r\nattractive exterior; only she had a fickle disposition. But that is no\r\ndetriment in the feminine sex; it even affords pleasure.... So, then, I\r\ntrudged to her porch--and that trip seemed very salt to me! Well, I\r\nthought, Nimfod\xc3\xb3ra Semy\xc3\xb3novna will regale me with bilberry-water, and\r\nother refreshments--and I had already grasped the door-handle when,\r\nsuddenly, around the corner of the servants\xe2\x80\x99 cottage there arose a\r\ntrampling of feet, a squealing and shouting of small boys.... I looked\r\nround. O Lord, my God! Straight toward me was dashing a huge, reddish\r\nbeast, which at first sight I did not recognise as a dog; its jaws were\r\ngaping, its eyes were blood-shot, its hair stood on end.... Before I\r\ncould take breath the monster leaped upon the porch, elevated itself on\r\nits hind legs, and fell straight on my breast. What do you think of that\r\nsituation? I was swooning with fright, and could not lift my arms; I was\r\ncompletely stupefied; ... all I could see were the white tusks right at\r\nthe end of my nose, the red tongue all swathed in foam. But at that\r\nmoment another dark body soared through the air in front of me, like a\r\nball--it was my darling Tres\xc3\xb3r coming to my rescue; and she went at\r\nthat beast\xe2\x80\x99s throat like a leech! The beast rattled hoarsely in the\r\nthroat, gnashed its teeth, staggered back.... With one jerk I tore open\r\nthe door, and found myself in the anteroom. I stood there, beside myself\r\nwith terror, threw my whole body against the lock, and listened to a\r\ndesperate battle which was in progress on the porch. I began to shout,\r\nto call for help; every one in the house took alarm. Nimfod\xc3\xb3ra\r\nSemy\xc3\xb3novna ran up with hair unbraided; voices clamoured in the\r\ncourtyard--and suddenly there came a cry: \xe2\x80\x9cHold him, hold him, lock the\r\ngate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI opened the door,--just a crack,--and looked. The monster was no longer\r\non the porch. People were rushing in disorder about the courtyard,\r\nflourishing their arms, picking up billets of wood from the ground--just\r\nas though they had gone mad. \xe2\x80\x9cTo the village! It has run to the\r\nvillage!\xe2\x80\x9d shrieked shrilly a peasant-woman in a pointed coronet\r\nhead-dress of unusual dimensions, thrusting her head through a\r\ngarret-window. I emerged from the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is Tres\xc3\xb3r?\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.--And at that moment I caught sight of my\r\nsaviour. She was walking away from the gate, limping, all bitten, and\r\ncovered with blood....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what was it, after all?\xe2\x80\x9d--I asked the people, as they went circling\r\nround the courtyard like crazy folk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA mad dog!\xe2\x80\x9d--they answered me, \xe2\x80\x9cbelonging to the Count; it has been\r\nroving about here since yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe had a neighbour, a Count; he had introduced some very dreadful dogs\r\nfrom over-sea. My knees gave way beneath me; I hastened to the mirror\r\nand looked to see whether I had been bitten. No; God be thanked, nothing\r\nwas visible; only, naturally, my face was all green; but Nimfod\xc3\xb3ra\r\nSemy\xc3\xb3novna was lying on the couch, and clucking like a hen. And that was\r\neasily to be understood: in the first place, nerves; in the second\r\nplace, sensibility. But she came to herself, and asked me in a very\r\nlanguid way: was I alive? I told her that I was, and that Tres\xc3\xb3r was my\r\nsaviour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAkh,\xe2\x80\x9d--said she,--\xe2\x80\x9cwhat nobility! And I suppose the mad dog smothered\r\nher?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I,--\xe2\x80\x9cit did not smother her, but it wounded her seriously.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAkh,\xe2\x80\x9d--said she,--\xe2\x80\x9cin that case, she must be shot this very moment!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing of the sort,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I;--\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t agree to that; I shall try to\r\ncure her.\xe2\x80\x9d ...\r\n\r\nIn the meanwhile, Tres\xc3\xb3r began to scratch at the door; I started to open\r\nit for her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAkh,\xe2\x80\x9d--cried she,--\xe2\x80\x9cwhat are you doing? Why, she will bite us all\r\ndreadfully!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I,--\xe2\x80\x9cthe poison does not take effect so soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAkh,\xe2\x80\x9d--said she,--\xe2\x80\x9chow is that possible? Why, you have gone out of your\r\nmind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNimf\xc3\xb3tchka,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I,--\xe2\x80\x9ccalm thyself; listen to reason....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut all at once she began to scream: \xe2\x80\x9cGo away; go away this instant with\r\nyour disgusting dog!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInstantly,\xe2\x80\x9d--said she,--\xe2\x80\x9cthis very second! Take thyself off,\r\nbrigand,\xe2\x80\x9d--said she,--\xe2\x80\x9cand don\xe2\x80\x99t dare ever to show yourself in my sight\r\nagain. Thou mightest go mad thyself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d--said I; \xe2\x80\x9conly give me an equipage, for I am afraid\r\nto go home on foot now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe riveted her eyes on me. \xe2\x80\x9cGive, give him a calash, a carriage, a\r\ndrozhky, whatever he wants,--anything, for the sake of getting rid of\r\nhim as quickly as possible. Akh, what eyes! akh, what eyes he has!\xe2\x80\x9d--And\r\nwith these words she flew out of the room, dealing a maid who was\r\nentering a box on the ear,--and I heard her go off into another fit of\r\nhysterics.--And you may believe me or not, gentlemen, but from that day\r\nforth I broke off all acquaintance with Nimfod\xc3\xb3ra Semy\xc3\xb3novna; and,\r\ntaking all things into mature consideration, I cannot but add that for\r\nthat circumstance also I owe my friend Tres\xc3\xb3r a debt of gratitude until\r\nI lie down in my coffin.\r\n\r\nWell, sir, I ordered a calash to be harnessed, placed Tres\xc3\xb3r in it, and\r\ndrove off home with her. At home I looked her over, washed her wounds,\r\nand thought to myself: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take her to-morrow, as soon as it is light,\r\nto the wizard in Efr\xc3\xa9m County. Now this wizard was an old peasant, a\r\nwonderful man; he would whisper over water--but others say that he\r\nemitted serpents\xe2\x80\x99 venom on it--and give it to you to drink, and your\r\nmalady would instantly disappear. By the way, I thought, I\xe2\x80\x99ll get myself\r\nbled in Efr\xc3\xa9movo; \xe2\x80\x99tis a good remedy for terror; only, of course, not\r\nfrom the arm, but from the bleeding-vein.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut where is that place--the bleeding-vein?\xe2\x80\x9d--inquired Finopl\xc3\xa9ntoff,\r\nwith bashful curiosity.\r\n\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know? That spot on the fist close to the thumb, on which one\r\nshakes snuff from the horn.--Just here, see! \xe2\x80\x99Tis the very best place\r\nfor blood-letting; therefore, judge for yourselves; from the arm it will\r\nbe venal blood, while from this spot it is sparkling. The doctors don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow that, and don\xe2\x80\x99t understand it; how should they, the sluggards, the\r\ndumb idiots? Blacksmiths chiefly make use of it. And what skilful\r\nfellows they are! They\xe2\x80\x99ll place their chisel on the spot, give it a\r\nwhack with their hammer--and the deed is done!... Well, sir, while I was\r\nmeditating in this wise, it had grown entirely dark out of doors, and it\r\nwas time to go to sleep. I lay down on my bed, and Tres\xc3\xb3r, of course,\r\nwas there also. But whether it was because of my fright or of the\r\nstifling heat, or because the fleas or my thoughts were bothersome, at\r\nany rate, I could not get to sleep. Such distress fell upon me as it is\r\nimpossible to describe; and I kept drinking water, and opening the\r\nwindow, and thrumming the \xe2\x80\x9cKam\xc3\xa1rynskaya\xe2\x80\x9d[46] on the guitar, with Italian\r\nvariations.... In vain! I felt impelled to leave the room,--and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nall there was to it. At last I made up my mind. I took a pillow, a\r\ncoverlet, and a sheet, and wended my way across the garden to the\r\nhay-barn; well, and there I settled myself. And there things were\r\nagreeable to me, gentlemen; the night was still, extremely still, only\r\nnow and then a breeze as soft as a woman\xe2\x80\x99s hand would blow across my\r\ncheek, and it was very cool; the hay was fragrant as tea, the katydids\r\nwere rasping in the apple-trees; then suddenly a quail would emit its\r\ncall--and you would feel that he was taking his ease, the scamp, sitting\r\nin the dew with his mate.... And the sky was so magnificent; the stars\r\nwere twinkling, and sometimes a little cloud, as white as wadding, would\r\nfloat past, and even it would hardly stir....\r\n\r\nAt this point in the narrative, Skvor\xc3\xa9vitch sneezed; Kinar\xc3\xa9vitch, who\r\nnever lagged behind his comrade in anything, sneezed also. Ant\xc3\xb3n\r\nStep\xc3\xa1nitch cast a glance of approbation at both.\r\n\r\nWell, sir--[went on Porf\xc3\xadry Kap\xc3\xadtonitch],--so I lay there, and still I\r\ncould not get to sleep. A fit of meditation had seized upon me; and I\r\npondered chiefly over the great marvel, how that Prokh\xc3\xb3ritch had rightly\r\nexplained to me about the warning--and why such wonders should happen to\r\nme in particular.... I was astonished, in fact, because I could not\r\nunderstand it at all--while Tres\xc3\xb3rushko whimpered as she curled herself\r\nup on the hay; her wounds were paining her. And I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you another\r\nthing that kept me from sleeping--you will hardly believe it; the moon!\r\nIt stood right in front of me, so round and big and yellow and flat; and\r\nit seemed to me as though it were staring at me--by Heaven it did; and\r\nso arrogantly, importunately.... At last I stuck my tongue out at it, I\r\nreally did. Come, I thought, what art thou so curious about? I turned\r\naway from it; but it crawled into my ear, it illuminated the back of my\r\nhead, and flooded me as though with rain; I opened my eyes, and what did\r\nI see? It made every blade of grass, every wretched little blade in the\r\nhay, the most insignificant spider\xe2\x80\x99s web, stand out distinctly! \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\r\nlook, then!\xe2\x80\x9d said I. There was no help for it. I propped my head on my\r\nhand and began to stare at it. But I could not keep it up; if you will\r\nbelieve it, my eyes began to stick out like a hare\xe2\x80\x99s and to open very\r\nwide indeed, just as though they did not know what sleep was like. I\r\nthink I could have eaten up everything with those same eyes. The gate\r\nof the hay-barn stood wide open; I could see for a distance of five\r\nversts out on the plain; and distinctly, not in the usual way on a\r\nmoonlight night. So I gazed and gazed, and did not even wink.... And\r\nsuddenly it seemed to me as though something were waving about far, far\r\naway ... exactly as though things were glimmering indistinctly before my\r\neyes. Some time elapsed; again a shadow leaped across my vision,--a\r\nlittle nearer now; then again, still nearer. What is it? I thought. Can\r\nit be a hare? No, I thought, it is larger than a hare, and its gait is\r\nunlike that of a hare. I continued to look, and again the shadow showed\r\nitself, and it was moving now across the pasture-land (and the\r\npasture-land was whitish from the moonlight) like a very large spot; it\r\nwas plain that it was some sort of a wild beast--a fox or a wolf. My\r\nheart contracted within me ... but what was I afraid of, after all?\r\nAren\xe2\x80\x99t there plenty of wild animals running about the fields by night?\r\nBut my curiosity was stronger than my fears; I rose up, opened my eyes\r\nvery wide, and suddenly turned cold all over. I fairly froze rigid on\r\nthe spot, as though I had been buried in ice up to my ears; and why? The\r\nLord only knows! And I saw the shadow growing bigger and bigger, which\r\nmeant that it was making straight for the hay-barn.... And then it\r\nbecame apparent to me that it really was a large, big-headed wild\r\nbeast.... It dashed onward like a whirlwind, like a bullet.... Good\r\nheavens! What was it? Suddenly it stopped short, as though it scented\r\nsomething.... Why, it was the mad dog I had encountered that day! \xe2\x80\x99Twas\r\nhe, \xe2\x80\x99twas he! O Lord! And I could not stir a finger, I could not\r\nshout.... It ran to the gate, glared about with its eyes, emitted a\r\nhowl, and dashed straight for me on the hay!\r\n\r\nBut out of the hay, like a lion, sprang my Tres\xc3\xb3r; and then the struggle\r\nbegan. The two clinched jaw to jaw, and rolled over the ground in a\r\nball! What took place further I do not remember; all I do remember is\r\nthat I flew head over heels across them, just as I was, into the garden,\r\ninto the house, and into my own bedroom!... I almost dived under the\r\nbed--there\xe2\x80\x99s no use in concealing the fact. And what leaps, what bounds\r\nI made in the garden! You would have taken me for the leading ballerina\r\nwho dances before the Emperor Napoleon on the day of his Angel--and even\r\nshe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have overtaken me. But when I had recovered myself a\r\nlittle, I immediately routed out the entire household; I ordered them\r\nall to arm themselves, and I myself took a sword and a revolver. (I must\r\nconfess that I had purchased that revolver after the Emancipation, in\r\ncase of need, you know--only I had hit upon such a beast of a pedlar\r\nthat out of three charges two inevitably missed fire.) Well, sir, I\r\ntook all this, and in this guise we sallied forth, in a regular horde,\r\nwith staves and lanterns, and directed our footsteps toward the\r\nhay-barn. We reached it and called--nothing was to be heard; we entered\r\nthe barn at last.... and what did we see? My poor Tres\xc3\xb3rushko lay dead,\r\nwith her throat slit, and that accursed beast had vanished without\r\nleaving a trace!\r\n\r\nThen, gentlemen, I began to bleat like a calf, and I will say it without\r\nshame; I fell down on the body of my twofold rescuer, so to speak, and\r\nkissed her head for a long time. And there I remained in that attitude\r\nuntil my old housekeeper, Prask\xc3\xb3vya, brought me to my senses (she also\r\nhad run out at the uproar).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you grieve so over the dog, Porf\xc3\xadry Step\xc3\xa1nitch?\xe2\x80\x9d--said she. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nwill surely catch cold, which God forbid!\xe2\x80\x9d (I was very lightly clad.)\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if that dog lost her life in saving you, she ought to reckon it as\r\na great favour!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlthough I did not agree with Prask\xc3\xb3vya, I went back to the house. And\r\nthe mad dog was shot on the following day by a soldier from the\r\ngarrison. And it must have been that that was the end appointed by Fate\r\nto the dog, for the soldier fired a gun for the first time in his life,\r\nalthough he had a medal for service in the year \xe2\x80\x9912. So that is the\r\nsupernatural occurrence which happened to me.\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE narrator ceased speaking and began to fill his pipe. But we all\r\nexchanged glances of surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut perhaps you lead a very upright life,\xe2\x80\x9d--began Mr.\r\nFinopl\xc3\xa9ntoff,--\xe2\x80\x9cand so by way of reward....\xe2\x80\x9d But at that word he\r\nfaltered, for he saw that Porf\xc3\xadry Kap\xc3\xadtonitch\xe2\x80\x99s cheeks were beginning to\r\nswell out and turn red, and his eyes too were beginning to pucker\r\nup--evidently the man was on the point of breaking out....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut admitting the possibility of the supernatural, the possibility of\r\nits interference in everyday life, so to speak,\xe2\x80\x9d--began Ant\xc3\xb3n\r\nStep\xc3\xa1nitch:--\xe2\x80\x9cthen what r\xc3\xb4le, after this, must sound sense play?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNone of us found any answer, and, as before, we remained perplexed.\r\n\r\n\r\nFOOTNOTES:\r\n\r\n[1] The well-known poet Y\xc3\xa1koff Petr\xc3\xb3vitch Pol\xc3\xb3nsky is the authority for\r\nthis statement, in his \xe2\x80\x9cRecollections of Turg\xc3\xa9nieff,\xe2\x80\x9d printed in the\r\nearly numbers of the _Niva_ for 1884.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[2] The finest of the public parks in Moscow, situated near the famous\r\nSparrow Hills, is called \xe2\x80\x9cNesk\xc3\xbatchny\xe2\x80\x9d--\xe2\x80\x9cNot Tiresome,\xe2\x80\x9d generally\r\nrendered \xe2\x80\x9cSans Souci.\xe2\x80\x9d It contains an imperial residence, the Alexander\r\nPalace, used as an official summer home by the Governor-General of\r\nMoscow.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[3] Princes, princesses, counts, and countesses have the title of\r\n_Siy\xc3\xa1telstvo_ (_siy\xc3\xa1m_--to shine, to be radiant); generally translated\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIllustrious Highness\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cSerenity.\xe2\x80\x9d--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[4] The custom still prevails in Russia, to a great extent, for all\r\nelderly women to wear caps. In the peasant class it is considered\r\nas extremely indecorous to go \xe2\x80\x9csimple-haired,\xe2\x80\x9d as the expression\r\nruns--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[5] The famous gate from the \xe2\x80\x9cWhite town\xe2\x80\x9d into the \xe2\x80\x9cChina town,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nin Moscow, where there is a renowned holy picture of the Iberian\r\nVirgin, in a chapel. Evidently the lawyers\xe2\x80\x99 quarter was in this\r\nvicinity.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[6] In P\xc3\xbashkin\xe2\x80\x99s poem, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Gipsies.\xe2\x80\x9d--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[7] The respectful \xe2\x80\x9cs,\xe2\x80\x9d which is an abbreviation of \xe2\x80\x9csir\xe2\x80\x9d or\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmadam.\xe2\x80\x9d--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[8] A square in Moscow.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[9] A great plain situated on the outskirts of the town. So called\r\nbecause (says tradition) it was here that annually were assembled\r\nthe young girls who were sent, in addition to the money tribute, to\r\nthe Khan, during the Tat\xc3\xa1r period, in the thirteenth and fourteenth\r\ncenturies.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[10] Afan\xc3\xa1sy Afan\xc3\xa1sievitch Sh\xc3\xa9nshin (1820-1892) always wrote under this\r\nname.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[11] A verst is two thirds of a mile.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[12] The Name-day--that is, the day of the saint after whom a person\r\nis named--is observed with feasting and congratulation, instead of the\r\nbirthday. For ceremonious calls, no matter at what hour of the day, a\r\nman who has no official uniform must wear his evening suit, on penalty\r\nof being considered ignorant or rude, or (in official circles) of being\r\nrefused admittance.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[13] A pun is intended: _ispr\xc3\xa1vno_, regularly, in orderly manner;\r\n_ispravnik_, the chief of police in a rural district.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[14] Male serfs. The women and children did not figure on the revision\r\nlists.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[15] Of different grades (civil and military), for the children of the\r\nnobility or gentry. They are not charities.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[16] In those days there was a great difference in the value of silver\r\nand paper money--hence the kind is usually specified.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[17] Literally, \xe2\x80\x9cdear little mother.\xe2\x80\x9d--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[18] A little Russian song.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[19] The popular nickname among Great Russians for the Little\r\nRussians.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[20] Literally, \xe2\x80\x9cdear little father\xe2\x80\x9d: the genuinely Russian mode of\r\naddress to a man of any class, as _m\xc3\xa1tushka_ (\xe2\x80\x9cdear little mother\xe2\x80\x9d) is\r\nfor women of all classes.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[21] The poem, after describing the deadly qualities of the upas-tree,\r\nnarrates how a potentate sent one of his slaves to bring him flowers\r\nfrom it. The slave, thoroughly aware of his danger, fulfilled his\r\nsovereign\xe2\x80\x99s behest, returned with branches of the tree, and dropped\r\ndead.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[22] It should be Ak\xc3\xadm, popular for Iak\xc3\xadnthos, Hyacinth.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[23] The unpoetical Russian name is \xe2\x80\x9cchicken-blindness\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(night-blindness).--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[24] The eighth (out of fourteen) in Peter the Great\xe2\x80\x99s Table of\r\nRanks.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[25] \xe2\x80\x9cS\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d a polite addition to sentences, equivalent to a contraction\r\nof the Nacht.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n[34] This is in English in the original.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[35] The fifth (from the top) of the fourteen grades in the Table of\r\nRanks, instituted by Peter the Great, having come into use after\r\nthe schism. Later on they invented curious religious reasons for their\r\ndenunciation of these and other things.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[38] The holy picture (_ik\xc3\xb3na_) of the Mother of Christ.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[39] The Old Ritualists\xe2\x80\x99 most opprobrious epithet, designating a member\r\nof the State Church, which accepted the emendations instituted by\r\nPatriarch N\xc3\xadkon referred to in a previous note.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[40] One of the hotly disputed points of difference between the Old\r\nRitualists and the members of the State Church is in their manner of\r\ncrossing themselves. The latter use the forefinger, middle finger, and\r\nthumb joined at the tips.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[41] In the government of Tula, central Russia.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[42] Formerly, houses were not numbered, and addresses ran: \xe2\x80\x9cIn\r\nthe house of ***\xe2\x80\x9d (the proprietor, man or woman), often with many\r\ncomplicated directions added to designate the special house.\r\nThese ancient addresses still remain, along with the numbers or\r\nalone, especially on many of the houses in Moscow, and in country\r\ntowns.--TRANSLATOR.\r\n\r\n[43] Old Ritualists will tolerate no others.
